Preface
This thesis was written at the Department of Electrical Engineering as a part of the re-
quirements for obtaining the master’s degree in Biomedical Engineering at the Technical
University of Denmark (DTU). The project deals with the signal processing relevant for im-
plementing a Brain Compute Interface system as a potential supplementary tool for stroke
rehabilitation. The project was developed from January to June, 2017, in collaboration
with the university and Rigshospitalet-Glostrup.
Lyngby, 22 June 2017

And last, but not least, a heartfelt thank you to friends and family, who stoically listened
to all the little complaints and victories for the past six months and without which, I am
certain, I would not be here today.

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Worldwide, stroke is one of the leading causes of death and long-term disability. According
to the WHO, it aﬀects around 11 million people per year, out of which approximately one
third is left with some degree of permanent impairment.
These numbers underline the
importance of optimizing and improving the current rehabilitation methods. One emerging
approach to do so entails the use of Brain-Computer Interface (BCI) systems, as studies
demonstrate that motor imagery (MI) has the capability to induce neuroplasticity, thereby
improving the recovery of the damaged areas.
One of the disadvantages of many BCI
systems, however, is the need for long calibration sessions, where no feedback is given to
the user. This is particularly undesirable if such a system is applied for stroke rehabilitation,
since it is important to guide the brain’s recovery in the right direction from an early stage.
In this project, we propose an option to tackle this problem: a sample-wise adaptive version
of a popular spatial ﬁlter, Common Spatial Patterns (CSP), was implemented using a
Recursive Least Squares (RLS) approach to update the ﬁlter coeﬃcients. It is a potential
tool for elimination or reduction of training sessions and is ideal for dimensionality reduction
and source identiﬁcation of MI-based BCI systems using Electroencephalogram (EEG). The
spatial ﬁlter was combined with a Regularized Discriminant Analysis classiﬁer to classify
the user’s intent and a regularized version of the CSP ﬁlter was employed on the shorter
training sessions to robustify it.
To test the performance of this algorithm, two diﬀerent datasets were employed. The ﬁrst
is a 4-class MI problem originating from the BCI competition IV, consisting of movements
from diﬀerent body parts and including long training sessions. The adaptive CSP (ACSP)
algorithm was assessed in a subject-dependent fashion and did not perform signiﬁcantly
better than its non-adaptive counterpart. Finally, the algorithm was tested on a 3-class
problem (our own data) consisting of diﬀerent motor tasks from the same limb, including
MI and motor execution (ME), to investigate the feasibility of a personalized rehabilitation
system for stroke patients. To that end, EEG data from 14 healthy subjects was recorded
for a short training session.
An online session with real-time feedback was carried out
where both adaptive and non-adaptive versions of the CSP ﬁlters were employed. The
results indicate signiﬁcantly better performances from ACSP: for the MI task, a kappa
value of 0.474 was achieved, as opposed to 0.113 from the non-adaptive CSP and the ME
task obtained an average kappa from the the ACSP of 0.420 compared to 0.267 of the
non-adaptive CSP. This means that the adaptive version increased the performance of the
simple CSP by 4-fold in the MI case and by 1.5-fold for the ME.
Overall, the ACSP ﬁlter proved to be of signiﬁcant value for small training sessions, even for
more complex tasks such as distinguishing movements from the same limb, which is a step
towards personalizing BCI-based stroke rehabilitation methods. Further improvements are
necessary to achieve higher accuracies and for such a system to be implemented in practice.

Schematic representation of class separation done by LDA. Observations are
depicted from 2 populations (blue and green). LDA projects the data in a
direction such that the distance between the projected means (µ1 and µ2) is
maximized at the same time that the within-class variances (s1 and s2) are
minimized. Figure modiﬁed from [119] . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
According to the WHO, stroke is one of the leading causes of both death as well as long-term
disability in the world [1]. Its symptoms are various degrees of cognitive, emotional or mo-
tor disabilities, with the most common motor impairment being hemiplegia or hemiparesis
(total or partial loss of movement on one side of the body). The current standard reha-
bilitation treatment consists mainly of physical therapy for motor recovery. Nonetheless,
studies indicate that 50-60% of stroke survivors are left with life-long motor disabilities -
which is why it is extremely important to optimize and enhance the prevailing rehabilitation
methods.
One of such improvements entails the use of MI-based BCI to assist classical rehabilitation
treatments. The supporting argument is that the neural pathways and regions of the central
nervous system (CNS) responsible for actual movement are also activated during MI. The
BCI system provides positive feedback to the user, thereby guiding and promoting the
natural neuroplasticity responsible for the motor recovery and eﬀectively encouraging the
re-learning of the lost functions by the CNS.
One of the BCI setups being currently investigated encompasses the use of a Functional
Electrical Stimulation (FES) system as a means to provide feedback to the subject in
the form of stimulation of the aﬀected limb.
BCI-FES systems are potentially cheaper
alternatives or supplements to current therapy; indeed, preliminary results have shown
both its feasibility as well as its superiority when compared to classical approaches.
Nevertheless, it is important to improve the current BCI paradigms in order to achieve
better and more suitable solutions. Particularly, one of the common limitations of BCI
systems is the user dependence: often a long calibration session without feedback is neces-
sary to compute user speciﬁc parameters, which is particularly undesirable in the case of
stroke patients since it is important to ensure positive neuroplasticity as early as possible.
One way to eliminate or decrease the length of this training session is through the use of
adaptive algorithms (either feature extraction, classiﬁcation or both).
1
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.2
Aim and Objectives
In this project, we propose a sample-wise adaptive feature extraction method: an extension
to the popular spatial ﬁlter Common Spatial Patterns (CSP) algorithm is made using a
Recursive Least Squares (RLS) approach. The adaptive step allows for automatic updates
of the ﬁlter coeﬃcients accounting, in principle, for the intra-subject non-stationarities of
the EEG signals, as well as enabling adaptation to inter-subject variabilities. Furthermore,
we design and implement a MI-BCI system capable of distinguishing between three diﬀerent
classes of upper limb movements (palmar grasp, pinch and elbow extension) which could
be used in a stroke rehabilitation scenario.
1.3
Thesis Contribution
The contribution of this thesis can be summed up as:
• Development of a sample-wise adaptive feature extraction method, namely an adap-
tive CSP (ACSP) ﬁlter using a RLS approach, and investigating its applicability in a
multiclass scenario (both in a user dependent and semi-independent situation, where
the calibration session is reduced).
• Designing and implementing a three class MI- and ME-BCI experiment consisting
of movements from the same upper limb, including both a training and an online-
feedback session.
• Using two diﬀerent datasets (our own and another belonging to BCI competition IV)
to evaluate and compare the performance of the proposed algorithms.
1.4
Thesis Organization
Chapter 2 presents the neurophysiological background necessary to understand the patho-
physiology of stroke, as well as its impact on the patient’s life and on the society; the current
rehabilitation methods are introduced along with the mechanisms behind it. In Chapter
3, the concept of BCI is introduced with emphasis given to the sensorimotor-based sys-
tems and its clinical applications on stroke rehabilitation. Chapter 4 includes a literature
review on the state of the art of signal processing approaches and BCI paradigms used in
stroke rehabilitation, with special attention given to adaptive spatial ﬁlters. Chapter 5 and
Chapter 6 describe the signal processing techniques used in this project and the datsets
used to test them, respectively. In Chapter 7 the results are presented Chapter 8 includes
the discussion of the results along with comparisons between the considered algorithms,
the diﬀerent training sizes and the MI and ME situations. Finally, Chapter 9 presents the
concluding assertions of the project.
2
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Clinical Background
2.1
Motor function
Since the focus of this project is on post-stroke motor rehabilitation, a brief introduction
on the physiological organization and control of motor function deems itself appropriate.
The neuromuscular system is responsible for the motor control and consists of motor units
(motor neurons and the muscle ﬁbers innervated by it), the spinal cord (where reﬂexes are
coordinated) and the descending pathways from brain stem circuitry, the cerebellum, the
basal ganglia and the motor cortex [2].
The upper motor neurons in the motor cortex project to the brain stem or spinal cord,
which then innervate the lower motor neurons which coordinate the muscle contractions.
In Fig. 2.1, a representation of the location of the sensorimotor cortices (posterior frontal
and anterior parietal areas) and the relative sections corresponding to muscle areas of the
body are shown. This anatomical knowledge is important to understand the origin of stroke
symptoms and how diﬀerent aﬀected areas can originate diﬀerent motor disabilities.
Finally, the upper motor neuron pathways are represented in Fig. 2.2.
3
==PAGE:18==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.1: Sensorimotor cortex. (a) Location of primary, premotor and supplementary
cortex on medial surface of brain. (b) “Motor homunculus”: Representation of the relative
size and location of the motor cortex areas responsible for the innervation of the several
muscles/sections of the human body [2].
Figure 2.2: Upper motor neuron pathways [2].
4
==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
2.2
Stroke pathophysiology
A stroke, also known as a cerebrovascular accident, is an acute focal neurologic deﬁciency
caused by an abnormal change in the blood ﬂow to the brain [2, 3]. There are two types
of stroke: ischemic and hemorrhagic. In the ﬁrst there is lack of oxygen supply in the
neurons, due to blockage of the cerebral vessels caused by thrombi or emboli, which causes
the neurons to die progressively. Ischemic stroke accounts for 87% of all strokes [2].
In the hemorrhagic stroke, there is rupture of one of the blood vessels and bleeding into
the brain tissue. Causes can be hypertension and aneurisma, among others [2]. The brain
hematoma sometimes puts great pressure on the surrounding tissue which rapidly leads to
coma and often death, making this type of stroke the most dangerous one [3].
In both types of stroke the brain’s ability to process information is highly distorted. Depend-
ing on the area of the brain which has been aﬀected, this can have striking consequences on
both cognitive, emotional and motor levels: some of the most common disabilities caused
by stroke include loss of vision and/or speech, confusion and paralysis, most commonly
one-sided [4].
2.2.1
Motor dysfunctions
Since this project is focused on motor rehabilitation, it is important to assess both the
speciﬁc neural regions responsible for these symptoms as well as the percentage of stroke
patients which are physically aﬀected and the severity of the disabilities.
Motor dysfunctions occur when the stroke has aﬀected a part of the corticospinal tract,
such as the motor cortex, posterior limb of the internal capsule, basis pontis, or medullary
pyramids [2]. This can cause hemiplegia or hemiparesis of the contralateral side of the lesion
(hemiparesis is a less severe form of hemiplegia where there is muscle weakness or partial
instead of total loss of movement). If the lesion is in the motor cortex the impairment will
most often be at the face and arm, excluding the leg since the neural region responsible for
leg movement is mostly innervated by a diﬀerent artery. A subcortical lesion, on the other
hand, will provoke identical weakness of face, arm and leg [2].
Following acute stroke a major part of the survivors will have been left with some form
of motor disorder. Of those, 20% will have regained some of the motor function in the
consecutive months which means that, of the patients that have survived a stroke, 50-
60% have life-long, chronic motor impairments with severe negative consequences on their
quality of life, such as dependence on external help and inability to perform daily activities
[4–6]. A study by Martins et al. [7], albeit with a relatively small population size, found
that 69% of these chronic stroke patients with severe motor impairments suﬀered from
hemiplegia and the rest from hemiparesis.
5
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
2.3
Epidemiology
Stroke is one of the global leading causes of death (according to the WHO it is the second
most common, being responsible for 11.9% of the deaths in 2012 [1]). It has a much higher
prevalence in developed countries and, even though evidence exists that its incidence has
either reached a plateau or decreased in Western countries over the last decade, largely
due to better lifestyle prevention measures like tighter control of high blood pressure and
reduced smoking, a rise can be expected due to the continued ageing of these populations.
This is because the incidence of stroke increases rapidly with age [8], with few cases under
the age of 40. Furthermore, as the lifestyle and society structure in low-income countries
rapidly changes, the incidence and mortality of stroke in these countries are also increasing
[9].
Moreover, stroke is the biggest single cause of long-term disability worldwide: in fact, out
of the approximate 15 million people suﬀering a stroke per year, one third dies and another
third is left with permanent disabilities, which can place an extraordinary burden on the
family and society [8, 9].
Furthermore, the cost of stroke should also be considered. For this, several factors need
to be taken into account: the aforementioned personal cost of the disability and loss of
quality of life for the individual and eventually also for the family; the healthcare costs on
the government; and ﬁnally the costs for the country in terms of loss of productivity [10].
While the ﬁrst is diﬃcult, if not impossible, to quantify, the last two costs can be estimated:
for example, in the Netherlands the average total cost of care for a patient during the six
months after a stroke was estimated to be around € 16 000 in 2003 and the total amount
spent on this disease to be around 3% of the whole healthcare budget. Around 65% of these
costs are considered “direct costs”, accounting for medical care and post-stroke therapy, and
the remaining are “indirect costs” relating to the loss of productivity [10]. In the U.S., the
yearly cost of the disease is approximately 33 billion dollars and it is expected to rise more
than ﬁve times this value by 2030 [11].
2.4
Post-stroke rehabilitation
The stated negative impacts of stroke in patient quality of life and society underpin the
necessity of eﬀective therapy methods. Currently the standard rehabilitation method for
post-stroke patients with motor disabilities is physical therapy for functional recovery [12].
This starts in the hospital, immediately after the patient stabilizes, which can be within 24
to 48 hours after the stroke onset. After some time the patient is discharged and therapy
continues at home or in dedicated medical facilities. The rehabilitation process is directed,
focused and repetitive on speciﬁc tasks, targeting the re-learning of speciﬁc motor skills
that are used in daily tasks to promote patient independence [13]. The higher the training
intensities (regarding volume and duration) the better are the rehabilitation results [14].
6
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
2.4.1
Principles behind post-stroke rehabilitation
There are two key principles in which current post-stroke rehabilitation methods are based
upon: the ﬁrst concerns brain plasticity and the second relies on the fact that progressive
and continuous motor practice is fundamental for physical rehabilitation at any time after
stroke [15].
2.4.1.1
Neuroplasticity
The ﬁrst principle is based on the increasing evidence that the human brain has a trait called
neuroplasticity. Neuroimaging and neurophysiologic studies as well as animal models have
demonstrated that the CNS adapts and changes in response to environmental stimuli as well
as interior stimuli throughout a person’s life, which means that synapses, neuronal networks,
connections and even number of neurons in the brain undergo changes as a means to adapt
and react to this uninterrupted ﬂow of information. This can for instance take the form of
sensory stimulation, during development or in situations of harm that damage or lead to
dysfunction of the nervous system. Short-term changes in behaviour and connections are
transformed into long-term ones. This process happens frequently in the neocortex region,
since it processes cognitive, sensory and motor information [16, 17].
Neuroplasticity has therefore been established as being the basis for maturing of the brain
(developmental plasticity), learning new skills (adaptive plasticity) as well as recovering
from a lesion (restorative plasticity). However, aberrant plasticity is also possible, where
the patterns of neuronal activation lead to abnormal behaviour [16, 18]. This is thought to
originate and support the symptoms in neuropsychiatric disorders [16].
Research has shown that after a stroke, which damages the neural tissue, there is extensive
CNS plasticity [19].
However, these plasticity-related changes can either be positive or
negative: they can lead to recovery of normal motor function but, in the case when the
subject repeatedly performs abnormal movements, plasticity can lead to solidiﬁcation of
these aberrant patterns, potentially even aggravating them [19]. A summary of the diﬀerent
neuronal changes is shown in Table 2.1
Rehabilitation procedures after stroke aim at enhancing the positive neuronal changes and
diminishing the negative processes. Several studies have been made to expand the knowl-
edge on how exactly the learning process happens in the brain, which could give great insight
on how the training processes should be directed in order to reach neurorehabilitation after
injury [19–21]. One important emerging concept is that stroke therapy, which enhances
neuroplasticity, has the possibility to make the stroke worse by decreasing the brain’s abil-
ity to deal with stress, if given too early in an acute stroke phase [21]. Studies have shown
that early classical therapy can cause detrimental eﬀects in rehabilitation, in some cases
actually increasing the infarction size [22, 23]. The exact time at which therapy should be
started is still a matter of debate, as diﬀerent studies indicate diﬀerent results (between 24
hours and 3-5 days within stroke onset). Evidence suggests that further research should
be made in order to ﬁnd the correct timing that promotes the best rehabilitation outcome
possible [21, 24].
7
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND

2.4.1.2
Progressive and continuous motor practice is fundamental for physical
rehabilitation at any time after stroke
While the degree of motor dysfunction depends on how the brain areas are aﬀected, it
is a fact that the repetition of certain motor activities are essential for physical recovery.
Furthermore, it is important that the therapy ensures the motivation and attention of
the subjects, in order to engage their neuronal learning networks [15]. Currently, physical
therapy is provided for stroke patients early after stroke, with strong scientiﬁc evidence
corroborating the success of such therapies. It is believed that the patients reach a plateau
and further improvement is impossible, therefore therapy often stops after the ﬁrst months.
2.4.2
Rehabilitation methods
Standard therapy includes ﬁtness and exercises for muscle strengthening, both in power
and resistance. Here we focus speciﬁcally in upper limb rehabilitation, as this is within the
scope of the project.
2.4.2.1
Upper limb rehabilitation
Rehabilitation therapy for the arms and hands normally starts with simpler movements
(one joint only, for example) and gradually moves to more complicated patterns, the goal
being to practise repeatedly a speciﬁc task relevant to everyday life, such as reaching and
grabbing a cup of coﬀee [15, 25]. Sometimes this is done while restraining the healthy limb
for a long time (commonly all day during two weeks, while 6 hours of progressive task related
exercises are made), a process known as constrain-induced movement therapy, CIMT. This
forces the healing process on the aﬀected brain hemisphere by increasing excitatory neural
activity.
However, this technique has been shown to work only in patients which have
8
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
maintained some degree of motor activity. Furthermore, evidence suggests that constraint
is not critical for recovery.
Another method shown to give similar results to CIMT is
bimanual practise, where both upper limbs are trained at the same time. Here the purpose
is to simultaneously activate both hemispheres of the motor cortex.
In cases where the hand is very weak an orthotic device with FES electrodes, which is
already commercially available, can be used to complement therapy and enable grasping
and other hand movements [15]. FES is an approach that takes advantage of the fact that
the peripheral muscles and nerves are not aﬀected, therefore it stimulates them to induce
contraction or relaxation of selected muscles and provoke the desired movement. So far it
has been used on upper and lower limb rehabilitation [15].
2.4.3
Limitations and alternatives of current rehabilitation therapy
The current rehabilitation process is demanding, challenging and laborious - fatigue is
a typical (and debilitating) symptom seen in chronic stroke patients. Additionally, stroke
survivors often experience a series of negative psychological traits or cognitive impairments,
such as depression, apathy (over 50% of patients manifest apathy one year after the stroke
onset [14]), self-doubt, denial of being ill, apraxia, among others. This means that the
necessary therapy intensities and duration for obtaining good rehabilitation results can be
very diﬃcult to maintain after the patients are discharged. Problems with maintenance
of activity are seen in many cases and in community-living patients physical activity is
very low [26]. Ultimately, four years after the stroke over 30% of the patients experience
persistent restrictions in terms of autonomy or engagement in society [27].
Moreover,
physical rehabilitation can carry an economic burden of the patient, as it involves a series
of healthcare facilitators.
For these reasons, recently diﬀerent rehabilitation strategies have started to be developed
and analysed, where the rehabilitation process is facilitated in order to increase patient
compliance. Some of these methods include neuromodulatory techniques such as the use
of repetitive transcranial magnetic stimulation (although its mechanisms are not fully un-
derstood) [28, 29] or the use of transcranial direct current stimulation [30]. Other methods
include the use of videogames and virtual reality to improve patient compliance and at-
tempt cost minimization [12]. More recently, treatment with stem cells and pharmacological
therapy have begun to be developed, as therapies aimed at redirecting the brain organiza-
tion after stroke [25]. Furthermore, the use of brain-computer interface (BCI) systems in
post-stroke rehabilitation has also been the subject of several studies in recent years. This
topic will be discussed in more detail in the following chapters.
9
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 3
BCI background
Brain-computer interface (BCI) technology allows for brain signals to be read and translated
into commands. The general principle is illustrated in Fig. 3.1: the brain signals are read
and ampliﬁed, then pre-processed for noise removal. Relevant features are extracted (often
followed by a process of selection, where dimensionality reduction is applied) and classiﬁed,
post processed and translated into a command. In online systems, the classiﬁcation is fed
back to the user through an user interface (multisensory feedback is possible, although
visual feedback is the most common), that being the method by which the user can “learn”
to control his or her brain signals. The output command can be used in a variety of diﬀerent
functions, depending on the purpose of the BCI: for example, cursor control, robot control,
prosthesis or wheelchair movement control, or to guide electrical stimulation of muscle or
brain tissues [25, 31].
Figure 3.1: General BCI block schematic. For each BCI application there is a variety of
options with advantages and limitations for each of the represented stages. Choosing the
better or most appropriate ones is the challenge and is what leads to better or poorer
results.
10
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 3. BCI BACKGROUND
3.1
BCI categories
Depending on how the brain signals are measured there are currently three possible BCI
platforms for clinical applications [31]. The ﬁrst involves invasive recording, where micro-
electrodes are applied into the cortex tissue and record a single unit, i.e., the ﬁring of a
single neuron. While this gives the highest possible spatial resolution it also represents a
greater risk and its long-term stability is unknown. Another category, which carries possi-
bly less risk, is classiﬁed as semi-invasive and uses electrodes placed mostly below the dura
that record signals from the surface of the cortex using electrocorticogram. Finally, the last
platform is non invasive and records signals from the scalp using mostly EEG (electroen-
cephalography). The non-invasive technique is easy to use, safe, inexpensive and has high
temporal resolution. However, it also has a much lower spatial resolution (at cm level), is
more sensitive to environmental noise and has a limited frequency range (less than ∼70 Hz)
[19].
3.1.1
EEG-based BCI systems
Table 3.1 summarizes the diﬀerent types of signals that can be recorded with non-invasive
methods, according to Chaudhary et al. [25].

11
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 3. BCI BACKGROUND
Of the signal types presented in Table 3.1, some are more commonly used than others
and are employed in diﬀerent applications. In this project, the BCI system is based on
sensorimotor rhythms and therefore this type of signal will be brieﬂy introduced.
3.1.1.1
Sensorimotor rhythms (SMR)
SMRs are tuned by motor intentions, both MI and ME. This modulation comprises a
decrease of amplitudes on the frequency band of the alpha rhythm, also called mu rhythm
(8-13 Hz), and beta rhythm (14-26 Hz), specially lower beta, and an increase in frequency
of the gamma rhythm (over 30 Hz), which tends to be more spatially focused than the other
two and is usually predominant in the primary sensory area [32, 33].
A change in amplitude, or power, of the EEG signal is directly related to the number of
synchronized neurons, i.e., an increase in amplitude indicates higher synchronization of the
underlying neuronal populations, and vice-versa [32, 33]. These are known as event-related
synchronization (ERS) and desynchronization (ERD). The movement-related ERD/ERS
can be decoded to determine the motor intentions of the subject and used to control multi-
dimensional applications.
Several studies indicate that imagined movement and actual movement, particularly com-
ponents corresponding to preparation of movement, show the same patterns of ERD/ERS,
which is an advantage while using MI for BCI systems [32, 34, 35]. These have been shown
to occur in localized sensorimotor areas corresponding to the part of the body that is being
used (refer back to the motor humunculus in Fig. 2.1). It has been shown that in imagined
movement there is ERD in the contralateral hemisphere and ERS in the ipsilateral hemi-
sphere of the motor cortex, the latter being associated with movement selection [31, 33,
36]. Typically the ERD complex has been applied in MI tasks.
3.2
Clinical applications
The two main BCI applications are assistive and rehabilitative BCIs [25].
Assistive applications comprise support in communication or movement to patients which
have partially or totally lost the ability to do so (i.e., replace lost functions). These include
Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis (ALS) patients or patients with CNS injury such as spinal
cord injury or brainstem stroke, which have permanently lost the ability to move, but not
the awareness or cognition.
Rehabilitative BCIs (or restorative or neurofeedback-based BCI) aim at redirecting brain
plasticity to improve motor rehabilitation in patients which have lost it due to, for instance,
stroke. This will be discussed in more detail in section 3.3.
12
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 3. BCI BACKGROUND
3.3
MI-BCI and stroke rehabilitation
3.3.1
Motor Imagery
MI is used in most studies regarding the employment of BCI for stroke rehabilitation, as it
is hypothesized that it promotes neuroplasticity related repair of the damaged brain areas
[12]. This is because it is now accepted that both imagining and executing an action are
based on the same principles [4]. There are behavioural similarities (for example, the time
necessary to execute both imaginary and actual movement has been proven to be the same,
a phenomenon known as mental isochrony) and similarities regarding neural pathways (the
neural system used to perform an action is activated during imagination of the action,
specially the parts related to planning and control of movements).
3.3.1.1
MI ability
A signiﬁcant limitation in studies involving BCI for stroke rehabilitation is the inclusion
of patients that are unable of producing MI signals. Indeed, de Vries and colleagues [4]
suggest that a considerable amount of stroke patients are impaired when it comes to MI
(around 18% in their study with 40 subjects, but reduced to 13% in the study by Ang and
colleagues [37] with 53 patients). It is also subject to discussion whether a stroke patient
can, after some time, regain some MI ability [38]. Therefore, it may be necessary to screen
the potential BCI users to assert whether or not they are BCI illiterate. De Vries and
colleagues [4] suggest approaches which can be used to make these assertions.
Ultimately, the explanation why some patients are unable to activate BCI with MI is not
yet known - Kasahara and colleagues [39] suggest that, in healthy subjects, it is correlated
to the level of development of their non-primary somatosensory and motor areas of the
cortex, but further research is necessary in order to conﬁrm and understand these ﬁndings.
3.3.2
Contribution of MI for stroke rehabilitation
The basis for MI-BCI systems for stroke-rehabilitation has been laid by studies reporting
an increase in motor cortex excitability as well as topographical changes after training [35,
40–44].
Furthermore, it is possible to explain the eﬀect of BCI therapy based on conventional
learning theories as well as by noting the motor learning-related changes happening in
the brains of healthy subjects following BCI use [45]. BCI training promotes learning by
reinforcement - the pairing of a stimulus with a positive or negative consequence is the
basic mechanism behind human learning. Such learning is promoted by synaptic plasticity,
enabled by repetitive transmission, and is assumed to be the same in healthy as well as
stroke-impaired brains. Behind BCI-induced learning there’s the mechanism of Hebbian
neural recovery, where the timing and amount of reinforcement are essential for eﬃciency
of the system [45].
13
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 3. BCI BACKGROUND
Preliminary results such as the one by Ramos-Murguialday and colleagues [46] or Ang and
colleagues [47] indicate the feasibility of incorporating BCI in post-stroke hand rehabilita-
tion as well as its improved results when compared to standard therapy coupled with MI
without neurofeedback. On the other side, many of the studies regarding MI-BCI in stroke
rehabilitation are performed on small samples. More large, randomized clinical trials are
necessary to conﬁrm the advantages and reliability of the method.
3.3.3
Advantages and limitations of using BCI in stroke rehabilitation
An advantage of complementing classical stroke motor therapy with BCI, besides the pre-
liminary results indicating better motor rehabilitation, is the cost reduction, as it elimi-
nates the need for constant presence of a physiotherapist [48]. Furthermore, it may increase
patient compliance due to improved results and, eventually, interesting or engaging user-
interfaces, as suggested by the work of Morone and colleagues [49]. A possible setback of
this therapy is the fact that most BCI systems require a long training time.
Ultimately, we need to take into account that this ﬁeld of study is fairly new and further
research is necessary in order to increase the knowledge on the subject and clarify certain
aspects so as to reach an optimized therapy strategy. Some of these unknown elements
include the length to which all patients are capable of producing MI signals and activate
the BCI system [19, 38], which features are the best for optimal results and how they
should be used, or what is the best and most eﬀective BCI format. For instance, studies
have shown poorer BCI performance in stroke patients when using current state-of-the-art
methods that, trained in healthy subjects, yield high accuracies [50], which indicates the
need for novel methods and further research with stroke patients. Additionally, evidence
suggests that the individual stroke characteristics lead to diﬀerent consequent neuroplastic
changes during recovery, which implies that for better results a BCI system should ideally be
tailored for each patient [51]. Furthermore, as stated by Nijboer et al., [52], it is important
that the future studies focus also on practical usability and not only on reliability, as mostly
happens at the moment.
14
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 4
State of the art
In this chapter a literature review on the state of the art of signal processing techniques and
BCI experiment paradigms is presented. The focus is both on multiclass MI-BCI, as such
is the goal of this project, and on MI-BCI paradigms used in stroke rehabilitation. Finally,
a review of the existing adaptive spatial ﬁlters used in MI-BCI systems is also presented.
4.1
Multiclass MI-BCI
Increasing the number of classes in a BCI system is desirable because it increases the degree
of control over a potential application. For this purpose, in SMR based BCIs, the most
obvious solution is to apply MI tasks that activate regions in the sensorimotor cortex as
distant as possible. This is done, for example, by imagining movement of the right and
left hand, or the tongue or feet. However, this is not an option when it comes to stroke
rehabilitation, as the goal lies with the activation of the motor pathways in themselves
and not the control of a disconnected device. Furthermore, most patients are hemiparetic
- therefore, the maximum possible amount of classes must be taken from the imaginary
movement of only one limb (the aﬀected one).
This is a notoriously harder task since
the related regions in the cortex are very close to each other and the so-called volume
conduction eﬀect distorts and mixes the electric signals as they travel from the cortex to
the scalp. This results in high spatial correlation between their signals, captured by the
EEG electrodes, and harder distinction between tasks [53].
For these reasons, the studies considered in this section include only research paradigms
where the classiﬁcation is made between more than 2 classes comprising movements of the
same limb. Among these studies there are those that distinguish between diﬀerent joints
(e.g. elbow and wrist) and movement of the joints and those that discriminate individual
ﬁnger movements. Since the latter is generally regarded as a more complicated task and
will not be implemented in this project, in section 4.1.1 some of the most relevant studies
pertaining only to the ﬁrst category are brieﬂy reviewed. Table 4.1 summarizes all of the
reviewed studies entailing both categories.
15
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
4.1.1
Discriminating between joints or movement type of same limb (arm
or hands)
This section is organized according to the diﬀerent paradigms found in the literature, per-
forming comparisons between the selected studies.
4.1.1.1
4-class wrist movement
A series of groups have attempted to discriminate 4 classes of wrist movement (ﬂexion,
extension, pronation and supination). Of these, Sepulveda and colleagues in 2004 [54] and
Vuckovic and Sepulveda in 2006 [55] are among the ﬁrst to do so. Sepulveda et al. had only
one subject and used the EEG signals prior to ME with 750 ms of duration, not performing
any classiﬁcation, while Vuckovic et al. performed the study on 5 healthy subjects using
MI and achieved approximately 80% oﬄine accuracies for both left and right hand.
Vuckovic et al. performed also a more complex pre-processing of the EEG signals: band-pass
ﬁltering using a 5th order Butterworth ﬁlter, re-referencing through a surface Laplacian with
the four nearest neighbours for enhancement of focal neural activity and ﬁnally Independent
Component Analysis (ICA) for artifact isolation and rejection. Sepulveda et al. tested
several ﬁlter types and concluded that an elliptical ﬁlter of order 8 would be optimal in
terms of computational cost vs. energy based Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) for the relevant
frequencies. These 2 studies employed diﬀerent features - the ﬁrst used 4 time-frequency
features: total band power (estimated using Welch’s method), dominant frequency within
each frequency band (alpha as in 5-15 Hz, beta as in 12-32 Hz and broadband as in 5-32
Hz), time-domain variance and ﬁnally the mean signal amplitude. In order to ﬁnd which
features in which channels would be more discriminative, the Davies Bouldin Index (DBI)
was used. The results, although hardly generalizable since they are produced from only
one subject, indicated that both motor and non-motor EEG channels were usable for class
discrimination and that the alpha band was the most used feature for classiﬁcation. On the
other hand, Vuckovic and Sepulveda used Gabor coeﬃcients calculated on the independent
components on 120 ms time windows and 2 Hz frequency bands for distinguishing between
MI classes. For dimensionality reduction, the DBI was also applied for 4 groups (4 frequency
bands: alpha, beta, lower gamma and higher gamma) and two dimensions (complex and real
values) and a recurrent Elman neural network was used as a classiﬁer. The results showed
that more than 70% of the features used in the classiﬁcation belonged to the gamma range.
More recently, in 2016, Edelman and colleagues [53] have attempted the same task. Their
approach diﬀered from the previous one in the sense that they performed source imaging,
i.e. converted the signals from the sensor (electrode) space to the source (cortex) space
by generating an EEG forward model that describes the electrical conductance from the
scalp sampling locations to the modeled dipole sources in the cortex. The group solved the
problem using the so-called weighted minimum norm estimate and the Colin27 MRI average
brain to create a conduction model of the scalp, skull and brain tissue through the boundary
element method (more details on the papers by the group [53, 56]). In their approach,
the group selected the regions of interest (ROI) using ICA. For the feature extraction
16
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
and selection, the authors obtained a time-frequency representation (TFR) of the selected
waveforms by convolving them with a Morlet wavelet of certain characteristics (again, see
[53] for further details). The authors claims that wavelets are a better method than Fourier
as they are capable of adjusting the width of the time windows to take similar number of
cycles of varying frequency components. The TFR’s were then split into 2 Hz frequency
bins and 250 ms windows (as these parameteres maximized classiﬁcation accuracy) and
used as features. Mahalanobis Distance (MD) was used for feature extraction and a MD-
based classiﬁer was used for classiﬁcation. The authors claimed that their method increases
the spatial resolution of the MI-BCI paradigm, as they achieved accuracies increased by
12.7% for overall classiﬁcation, compared to their own processing methods for sensor-space
classiﬁcation (which were similar except that all channels were used for feature extraction
instead of the ROIs).
4.1.1.2
2 to 3-class wrist or ﬁnger movements
Again, in this category, several groups have performed studies and achieved satisfactory
results. The ﬁrst one here reviewed is by Gu and colleagues in 2009 [57], which used the
planning phase of ME for classifying between extension or rotation of the wrist as well as
two speeds of movement (fast and slow), obtaining an average 2-class classiﬁcation error of
78.9%. The authors analyzed the movement-related cortical potentials (MRCPs), which are
slow cortical potentials. It is also modulated by motor intention and performance has been
proven to be as good a feature as SMR when it comes to discriminating between movement
intentions. As features, the MRCP rebound rate (determined by signal amplitude) was
used, as well as the mu and beta power obtained by discrete wavelet transform on Laplacian
ﬁltered data. The authors concluded that the tasks are easier to separate when performed
at diﬀerent speeds.
More recently, in 2015, Battacharyya and colleagues [58] attempted to optimize a 4-class
MI-BCI system by developing a new classiﬁer. Furthermore, a simpliﬁcation of the setup
was also performed by the use of an Emotiv headset for signal recording and 14 electrodes, a
relatively low number for multiclass classiﬁcation. The study achieved good accuracies (see
Table 4.1). In this paradigm, subjects performed MI according to visual cues on a screen for
wrist extension and ﬂexion and ﬁnger opening and closing, with auditory feedback during
the online phase. For noise reduction, an elliptical 12th order band-pass ﬁlter (8-25 Hz)
was applied, followed by Common Average Referencing (CAR) to reduce inﬂuence from
neighboring neural zones. As a feature, the Extreme Energy Ratio was used, as it is said
to have optimal discriminating capability. Finally, a new classiﬁer was applied (Interval
Type-2 Fuzzy Systems) which works on a 3-level hierarchical way, performing 3 binary
classiﬁcations: on the ﬁrst level it discriminates between rest state and MI state, on the
second level between wrist or ﬁnger MI and on the ﬁnal stage it distinguishes between
movement type (ﬂexion/extension or opening/closing). The authors defend that this new
method is superior to other more classical ones such as Support Vector Machines (SVM),
k-Nearest Neighbour or LDA and, additionally, that it has low computational cost, being
suited for online applications.
17
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
Finally in 2016 Jochumsen et al.
[59] have also developed a BCI system based on the
EEG data from the moments prior to movement. Here a 3-class system is implemented,
distinguishing between palmar, lateral and pinch grasps as they are used in ∼95% of all
daily activities. As temporal features, the mean amplitude of a sliding window was used
(both of the frequency range 0.01-5 Hz for MRCP as well as for 0.01-45 Hz for broadband
EEG) and as spectral features the average power of nine 5 Hz frequency bins occupying the
broadband was used. Sequential Forward Selection was compared to Principal Component
Analysis for feature extraction and classiﬁcation was performed by LDA. The authors noted
that the most discriminative features for movement detection were in the MRCP frequency
range, however for discrimination between movement types spectral features were best.
4.1.1.3
Classiﬁcation between diﬀerent joints (shoulder, elbow and wrist)
In this section, only the work by Yong and colleagues in 2015 [60] will be discussed, but for
details in other related studies, Table 4.1 can be consulted. The authors have attempted
to discriminate between rest, movement of the elbow joint (ﬂexion/extension) and ﬁngers
(grasp) in sets of binary classiﬁcations. They have also investigated the eﬀect of having a
simple task versus a goal-oriented task (elbow movement for reaching for a glass of water
and drinking it) in a setup for 3-class distinction (rest, MI-grasp, MI-elbow). They have
compared 3 feature extraction methods: CSP, Filter-Bank CSP (FBCSP) and Logarithmic
Band Power (LB). As features for the ﬁrst method, the log-variance of the EEG signals
between 7-30 Hz, projected using 6 diﬀerent spatial ﬁlters (the ones that most explain the
largest variance of class 1 and lowest variance of class 2 and vice-versa), was used. For
the second method, 3 frequency bands were applied: 7-15 Hz, 15-25 Hz and 25-30 Hz.
For the last method (LB), the features were the log-variance of the bandpass ﬁltered EEG
(7-30 Hz) from every channel. 3 classiﬁcation methods were also compared: LDA, Logistic
Regression with a fast Bayesian method and SVM with a radial basis function kernel. The
accuracy results indicated that the binary classiﬁcation was easier between rest and grasp
(∼80%) than between rest and elbow (∼75%) but poorer between grasp and elbow (∼61%).
However, a goal oriented task resulted in an improved accuracy of ∼5%, as seen with 3-class
classiﬁcation. In the end, the LB method outperforms the rest 41.7% of times, with FBCSP
close behind (39.3%). SVM was the best classiﬁcation method 46.4% of times.
4.2
MI-BCI paradigms used in stroke rehabilitation
All the studies described in section 6.2.2 have been developed and tested on healthy subjects.
However, taking into account the ﬁnal considerations of chapter 3, it is also important to
investigate how the BCI systems work in stroke patients. Furthermore, it is relevant to
review the current paradigms of MI-BCI therapy being applied.
Daly and Wolpaw [19] have classiﬁed the BCI setups for stroke rehabilitation in two cate-
gories: the ﬁrst strategy comprises of using EEG signals to give feedback to the users and
teach them to have more normal brain activity. In the latter, which is thought to increase
18
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
the rehabilitation beneﬁts, the goal is to use the brain signals of the patient, recorded with
EEG, to trigger a device that assists movement. This device can either be a robotic ap-
paratus attached to the patients arm that executes the imagined movement or can take
the form of FES. In the following section we analyze the studies implementing the second
strategy.
4.2.1
BCI-FES rehabilitation system
Several studies suggest that combining rehabilitation strategies with surface FES in the
upper limb results in improved results in terms of motor function, in patients with mild
or moderate motor impairment [19, 61]. Evidence indicates that positive results can be
obtained both in early (a few months) and late (over 1 year) stroke survivors [62]. In current
therapies, however, the application of FES is a passive process completely independent from
whether or not the correct brain signals are being produced [61].
Combining BCI with FES allows the FES system to be activated only when the desired
brain areas are active, through the use of a threshold. In this approach, the rehabilitation
is occurring in two ways [12]:
(a) Activation of central motor neuronal networks (due to the MI);
(b) By the sensorimotor feedback provided by the FES system on the aﬀected limb, which
is thought to also induce neuroplastic changes [61].
Preliminary results are promising: several small scale or case studies have proven the feasi-
bility of BCI-FES therapy, as well as its superior results with regards to FES therapy alone
[63–71].
In BCI-FES systems, two strategies are currently being implemented. Some systems are
based on movement observation (AOT - Action Observational Training) rather than classic
MI: in these cases, the FES system is activated based on the concentration level of the
subjects while watching movement of upper or lower limbs on a screen performing prede-
termined tasks. Studies suggest that observation of the movement that is intended to be
rehabilitated also induces cortex reorganization. Indeed, in the studies by Chung et al.[72],
Jang et al.[73] and Kim et al.[74], the patients receiving BCI-FES treatment during AOT
showed better motor improvements than patients receiving FES alone. In these studies,
the attention state is determined by a concentration index based on SMR, mid-beta and
theta rhythms amplitude changes and FES stimulation is given only when the concentration
index exceeds a threshold set during a training phase.
In this section, however, more emphasis will be given on the BCI setups that use actual MI
for the rehabilitation. Of these, the classiﬁcation is, to our knowledge, always binary, often
based on an adaptive threshold computed to lay between rest and MI state.
Starting in 2008, Daly and colleagues [65] implemented a small scale study where the feasi-
bility of BCI+FES/Robot in motor learning for stroke rehabilitation was laid. Here, three
classes were implemented both for ME and MI but classiﬁed binary: elbow/shoulder move-
ment, for which there was robot-aided movement as feedback, and hand/wrist movement,
19
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
for which there was FES feedback, and muscle relaxation. The authors found satisfactory
accuracies for the imposed motor tasks but observed that the imposed relaxation was a
much harder task for the subjects, underlying the importance of including relaxation tasks
in motor re-learning techniques, as it is also crucial for movement coordination.
Ramos-Murguialday and colleagues [46] in 2013 carried out a slightly larger scale study on
32 chronic stroke patients with severe hand paresis with no active ﬁnger extension, having
an orthosis for the hand or arm as an immediate feedback system. This would produce
the imagined movement in the experimental group and random movements in the control
group. Only the channels on the ipsilateral side of the lesion were considered, which makes
for harder BCI control but guarantees that the SMRs are being modulated on desirable
brain locations. Additionally, the paradigm involved reaching and grasping for an apple, as
the authors state that proximal training in the upper arm induces distal (hand) recovery
but the same does not always happen the other way around. The results were positive,
with signiﬁcant motor improvement on the experimental group, raising hope for chronic
stroke patients who are presently excluded from classical rehabilitation therapies.
Several other small scale studies have been reviewed which include BCI-FES systems for
stroke rehabilitation. The experimental paradigms are similar, distinguishing between rest
and MI of the aﬀected hand. Similarly, a low number of electrodes is employed in most
of the studies, so as to make them more feasible in real-life applications. The Fugl-Meyer
Motor Assessment and Action Research Arm Test scores are often used for motor assessment
of the patients and seem to generally increase with BCI-FES therapy. For a comparison
of some of the most relevant studies reviewed, including the signal processing techniques,
please refer to Table 4.2.
20

CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
4.3
Adaptive Spatial Filters
In a MI based BCI system, the task is to detect and distinguish between SMRs of diﬀerent
body parts. This can hardly be done using frequency information because most of the
time they overlap; furthermore, often it is even diﬃcult to distinguish between this type
of neurological activity and other signal sources, such as muscle artifacts or alpha activity
of the visual cortex. In fact, the most distinguishable characteristic of the SMRs is its
localization, as depicted in Fig.
2.1.
However, due to volume conduction, the spatial
resolution of the EEG electrodes is very low (around 5-9 cm according to [83]). This means
that the signal measured at each scalp location is a mixture from diﬀerent sources. For this
reason, it becomes highly relevant to apply some technique to retrieve the original source,
especially in the case of MI-BCIs. While there are various methods to do so, including
source analysis methods such as dipole ﬁt, a very commonly used strategy in BCI systems
is the employment of spatial ﬁlters, of which the CSP is one of the most widely known
methods. The idea of the CSP ﬁlter, which is explained in more detail in section 6.2.1, is
to project the original EEG data from sensor space into source space by maximizing the
variance of one class while minimizing it for the other.
There are, however, a few setbacks concerning the CSP algorithm, namely:
• It requires generally a large amount of data to avoid overﬁtting and generate robust
projecting vectors, and it is quite sensitive to outliers [84];
• It is typically subject-dependent and has no ability to adapt to the non-stationarities
that are characteristic of EEG signals, caused by changes in electrode impedances
or locations, subject concentration or fatigue level and other artifact sources such as
muscle activity or eye movements [85].
These characteristics of the algorithm therefore require long subject-dependent calibration
sessions for computing the ﬁlter, which is undesirable, and even then a drop in accuracy
from session to session is to be expected. Therefore, a series of variations of CSP have
subsequently been proposed to tackle these problems and aim at improve its performance
by making it robust against noise and non-stationary changes in the EEG data [86]. There
are two main approaches for doing so:
1. Extraction of invariant or robust features.
2. Adaptation to new data.
We will then discuss some of the relevant solutions found using both of these approaches.
4.3.1
Extraction of invariant or robust features
Lotte and Guan [87] have summarized the existing strategies for designing regularized CSPs
(RCSPs) and tested 11 diﬀerent algorithms on BCI competition data, concluding that
these can outperform a regular CSP by up to 10% in median classiﬁcation accuracy, lead
to more neurologically signiﬁcant patterns and enable eﬃcient inter-subject transfer. The
23
==PAGE:38==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
most promising algorithms were CSP with Tikhonov Regularization (TR) and Weighted
TR (WTR), both proposed by the authors. The ﬁrst approach consists of using TR to
constrain the solutions to ﬁlters with small norm and the latter adds higher weights to the
channels assumed to be more signiﬁcant. This approach is believed to be particularly useful
when it comes to avoiding overﬁtting when there is little training data. Another approach
useful for small training sizes involves regularizing the covariance matrix estimations with
Diagonal Loading, known to increase accuracy performances by up to 8 % [88].
Blankertz et al.
[89] have built a variant named as invariant CSP using features that
are invariant to the EEG non-stationarities. They do so by adding terms in the Rayleigh
coeﬃcient of the CSP algorithm that can be anticipated from prior knowledge, such as
the covariance matrix of ﬂuctuations in visual processing, and have succeeded in building
a proof-of-concept robust CSP against changes in the parietal alpha activity which can
simulate alterations in task vigilance.
In 2012 Samek and colleagues [86] have developed a stationary CSP with the goal of ex-
tracting stationary features. This is done by extracting ﬁlters that not only maximize or
minimize the variance for a certain condition but also attempt to maintain the variance
estimate along the projected direction stable throughout trials, using a proposed measure
of stationarity. The approach also takes into consideration chunks of the EEG signal to
account for slowly-varying changes such as shifts in attention and electrode impedance and
is allied with WTR to avoid overﬁtting. The authors compare their procedure with other
state-of-the-art methods and obtained satisfying results, concluding, as Lotte et al. [87]
and others have, that the regularization proves to be particularly useful for subjects with
low BCI eﬃciency.
Many other approaches to robustify CSP exist in the literature but we will mention only
one more, formulated by Sannelli and colleagues [90]. This group proposes a variant named
Common Spatial Pattern Patches, which is considered as compromise between Laplacian
ﬁlter and CSP, combining the advantages of both. It consists of applying the CSP algorithm
to small channel sets - the so-called patches - and combine the most relevant resulting
features using feature selection methods. By applying CSP on a smaller number of channels,
the risk of overﬁtting is reduced and, therefore, less training data is required.
4.3.2
Adaptation to new data
The methods pertaining to this approach focus on incorporating new data, i.e., handling
changes that occur in the EEG in sessions following the initial training one.
There are two possible ways to perform this adaptation: blockwise and samplewise. Block-
wise adaptation requires that the ﬁlter or classiﬁer is retrained after each block in the
recording session. This means that the adaptation is slower and there are no changes in the
system during each block. On the other hand, samplewise adaptation allows the system to
adapt and handle changes in real time, making it a more desirable system. Furthermore,
in real life applications, a samplewise adaptive system is, in principle, more independent.
We will now summarize examples of studies which have adopted both strategies.
24
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
4.3.2.1
Blockwise adaptation
In 2006, Sun and Zhang [91] proposed an adaptive unsupervised CSP algorithm through
a weighted update of the covariance matrices.
With each new coming trial, the class
covariance matrix are updated using a forgetting factor and the CSP is recalculated. The
use of past data allows for a certain robustness of the algorithm and the incorporation of
new data accounts for the EEG variability; nevertheless, recalculating the CSP for each trial
is a computationally expensive task. On their 3-class paradigm (left hand, right hand and
word imagination), the authors achieved 65.12% accuracy, which was signiﬁcantly better
than the standard ﬁxed CSP algorithm. A similar approach with a diﬀerent method for
updating the covariance matrices is tested by Chen et al. [92]. Also in 2006, Tomioka
and colleagues [93] developed another unsupervised blockwise adaptive CSP which requires
the computation of the new data covariance matrix but used a normalization approach to
obtain the adaptive spatial ﬁlter weights, on the assumption that the joint distribution of
the whitened signal remains unchanged.
In 2015, Song and Yoon [85] designed an adaptive 2-class algorithm where the spatial
ﬁlters are updated for each trial based on a similarity measure between new and training
data (three diﬀerent measures are used: feature variance-based distance, Kullback-Leibler
divergence and Frobenius norm). The authors tested non-accumulative vs. accumulative
implementation and concluded that the ﬁrst provided better results (i.e. not adding the
classiﬁed target trials with the training data).
Another paradigm is tested by Mondini and colleagues in 2016 [94] where the training and
testing sessions are joined together: the EEG session starts with 3 minutes where the CSP
weights and SVM classiﬁer are initialized (no feedback), which is automatically followed
by a longer session with feedback where the CSP and SVM are retrained using data only
from the correctly labelled signal portions out of 10 repetitions; once the classiﬁcation
accuracies are considered acceptable the system switches automatically to the ﬁnal mode,
where there is only feedback and no adaptation. Each subject participated in 6 sessions,
where the accuracies steadily increased. The authors have also implemented some strategies
during the training phase to reduce user frustration (adaptive thresholds for feedback and
unbalanced cues to challenge the user).
Finally, Sannelli et al. [84] have combined their robust BCI with the CSPs with a blockwise
online adaptive BCI system.
Their system allows the users to start receiving feedback
already in the ﬁrst session because the spatial ﬁlter and classiﬁer are calculated based on
data from 48 participants present on a database. In the 4 ﬁrst runs, there is supervised
adaptation where the parameters are adapted for each subject.
The LDA classiﬁer is
adapted after each trial by updating the class means and covariance matrices and the spatial
ﬁlter was recalculated using information from the last 60 trials. The ﬁnal run implements
unsupervised learning and is used as evaluation.
25
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 4. STATE OF THE ART
4.3.2.2
Samplewise adaptation
Mobaien and Boostani [95] presented in 2016 their adaptive version of a CSP. They were
inspired by the work of Lowne et al. [96], which had implemented an online sample-by-
sample adaptive self-paced real movement-based BCI using Extended Kalman Filter (EKF)
based dynamic logistic regression, and by the work of Tsui et al. [97], who implemented
an MI-based self-paced BCI system where the classiﬁer LDA is adapted on a trial-by-
trial basis through EKF-inspired equations. Mobaien and Boostani have implemented a
recursive adaptation method based on the EKF equations and compared it with normal
CSP and a version of it where the covariance matrices are updated incrementally as in the
work by Sun et al. [91], which the authors name Incremental Covariance Matrices (ICM)
CSP. The results showed overall increased classiﬁcation accuracies (not by a large margin)
for the proposed method compared to both normal and ICM CSP; the authors suggest this
adaptation method is superior to ICM since in the latter noisy trials are also learnt, while in
the EKF inspired CSP a weight vector is estimated which reduces the classiﬁer uncertainty.
Finally, it is worth mentioning here the axDAWN ﬁlter, presented by Woehrle et al. in
2015 [98]. The xDAWN ﬁlter is used for BCI systems relying on event-related potentials
such as the P300 potential, while the CSP is generally used for SMRs due to its ability to
discriminate between classes. Woehrle and colleagues have implemented an adaptive version
of xDAWN which uses generalized eigendecomposition, achieving samplewise incremental
training by updating the ﬁlter coeﬃcients through a RLS approach. The axDAWN perfomes
comparably to xDAWN when trained on a batch of data, but maintains the advantage
of incorporating new information in a samplewise manner. Furthermore, it requires low
memory space and is computationally eﬃcient.
26
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 5
Data and Experiment Description
The algorithm developed in this project was tested on two diﬀerent datasets. The ﬁrst one
belongs to the BCI competition, explained in section 5.1, and the second one, detailed in
section 5.2, was recorded in the context of this project.
5.1
4-class MI of diﬀerent body parts: BCI competition IV
dataset 2a
The purpose of the BCI competitions is to provide open-access high quality neuroscientiﬁc
data and promote the development of new paradigms and solutions for generally complex
tasks. The stipulation of guidelines for the solutions ensures that the results obtained by
the diﬀerent participants can be directly compared with each other.
The BCI competition IV in 2008 provided, among others, the dataset 2a, which consists of
four diﬀerent classes of MI. It was provided by the Institute for Knowledge Discovery from
the Graz University of Technology [99].
5.1.1
Experimental paradigm
The data is provided from two diﬀerent sessions, recorded on diﬀerent days: for the ﬁrst,
which we call calibration session, the labels of each trial are known. The second, named as
the evaluation session, is used to test the classiﬁer and evaluate the performance; therefore
the labels are assumed to be unknown.
Nine subjects participated in the experiment. The four MI classes are: left hand (class 1),
right hand (class 2), feet (class 3) and tongue (class 4). Each session comprised of 6 runs
separated by brief breaks and each run included 48 trials (12 for each class).
A representation of one trial is shown in Fig. 5.1. The trial starts with an acoustic warning
signal and a ﬁxation cross appears on screen. After 2 seconds, a cue is displayed for 1.25
27
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
seconds in the form of an arrow, indicating the start of the MI of one of the four classes
form the subject. After 4 seconds, a break of variable length was given in the form of a
black screen.
Figure 5.1: Illustration of the timing scheme of one trial of dataset 2a from BCI competition
IV. Figure taken from [99].
.
5.1.2
Data recording
The recording was made using 22 Ag/AgCl electrodes with 3.5 cm distance between them
(montage shown in Fig. 5.2). The monopolar signals were recorded having the left mastoid
as reference and the right as ground. The sampling frequency was 250 Hz and the EEG
signals were bandpass-ﬁltered from 0.5 to 100 Hz, along with a notch ﬁlter at 50 Hz to
reduce power line noise. Furthermore the sensitivity of the ampliﬁer was set to 100 µV.
Finally, three EOG channels were also recorded.
Figure 5.2: Electrode montage corresponding to the international 10-20 system used in the
data acquisition of dataset 2a of BCI competition IV. Figure taken from [99].
.
28
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
5.1.3
Participation requirements
The creators of the BCI competition have listed some requirements regarding the algorithms
used to classify this dataset, namely:
(a) A continuous classiﬁcation is provided for each sample in the form of class labels
(1,2,3 or 4);
(b) The algorithm is causal, which means that the classiﬁcation output at any time point
may only depend on current and past sample values;
(c) EOG artifacts are removed using the three provided EOG channels and signal pro-
cessing methods.
5.1.4
Online session simulation
To comply with the participation requirements, the continuous classiﬁcation output was
given by an online session simulation, which was performed for each trial according to the
following procedure:
1. A downsampling factor of 10 was applied to the data, since classifying every sample
is a laborious task. This means that one out of every 10 samples was classiﬁed.
2. Immediately after the cue presentation, the samples started to be classiﬁed. Each
incoming sample was concatenated to the previous ones until a window of 2 seconds
was created.
3. Once a classiﬁcation window of 2 seconds of data was obtained, it transformed into
a sliding window: each incoming sample was concatenated to the window while the
oldest sample was discarded. The classiﬁcation output was given until 4 seconds after
the cue presentation, when the task was ﬁnished.
4. At the end of the session, a label was given to the non-classiﬁed samples by mapping
them to the previously closest classiﬁed sample (which guarantees that the algorithm
remains causal).
The sample-wise adaptive algorithm was applied 0.5 seconds after the cue presentation, to
ensure that the adaptation occurred when the MI task was being performed. The EOG
artifacts were removed using a high-pass ﬁlter (cut-of frequency of 7 Hz).
5.1.5
BCI Competition IV Results
The results of the participants in the BCI competition for dataset 2a, taken from [100], are
summarized in Table 5.1.

These will serve as comparison for our own results, particularly the winner algorithm by
Ang et al. [101], named Filter-Bank CSP (FBCSP). The approach consists of ﬁltering the
EEG data prior to any processing using a ﬁlter bank of 9 distinct ﬁlter bands of width 4
Hz (namely 4-8, 8-12,..., 36-40 Hz). The data from each ﬁlter band is then projected in
the direction of maximum variance for each class trough a CSP and a mutual-information
based feature selection algorithm is used to select the 3 best features for each class. Finally,
four binary Naive Bayesian Parzen Window classiﬁers yielded the best performance of the
algorithm.
5.2
3-class MI and ME of single upper limb: our experiment
5.2.1
Experimental paradigm
The timing scheme of each trial was determined based on the analysis of experiments from
other studies on BCI using MI, such as [53, 58] as well as the dataset presented in the
previous section. The data was recorded in two diﬀerent days: on the ﬁrst day, a short
calibration session was recorded, where there was no feedback given to the subjects; on the
second recording day, the subjects were given feedback and the session was longer. Both
are described in more detail in the next subsections.
5.2.1.1
Calibration session
Initially, the subjects were instructed to sit comfortably in a chair which was placed in front
of the computer screen which displayed the interface. The layout of the experiment was
explained to them and they were asked to sign a participation form (see Appendix ??).
Each recording session comprises of two diﬀerent tasks: ME followed by MI. The reason
for having both is two-fold:
1. Having the subjects perform the actual movement before imagining them ensures the
researcher that the subjects will imagine the right movement, as they will have re-
peated it a series of times before. This is of the utmost importance in stroke patients,
as we know that the brain is recovering and the wrong stimulus can result in the
30
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
learning and consolidation of undesired movements (the negative side of neuroplas-
ticity).
2. It is worth to investigate whether in this situation the classiﬁcation of ME can be a
more successful task than that of MI.
The three motor tasks were performed with the same arm (the right one for consistency)
since stroke motor disabilities are generally one-sided, as seen in Chapter 2. The speciﬁc
movements were discussed with Dr. Helle K. Iversen, the co-supervisor of this project and
a medical doctor specialized in stroke rehabilitation, as being among the most indicated
for post-apopletic motor rehabilitation. It was decided to implement a goal-oriented visual
interface, i.e., show an object in the cue which the patients were supposed to imagine they
were handling as shown in the picture. This type of interface has been proven to improve
classiﬁcation results as, for some subjects, it is easier to imagine the motor task given a
speciﬁc objective [60]. Therefore, the three implemented classes, illustrated in Fig. 5.3, are:
• Palmar grasp (class 1): engaging all ﬁngers and palm to hold an imaginary object
between them. A hand holding a glass of water was used as goal-oriented cue (Fig.
7.12a).
• Pinch (class 2): collecting the ﬁngertips of the thumb, index and middle ﬁnger. The
picture used as cue depicts a hand pinching grains of salt (Fig. 7.16b).
• Elbow ﬂexion (class 3): ﬂexing the elbow while maintaining the wrist aligned with the
arm, with the thumb directed upwards/towards the subject as the forearm is lifted.
An arm in the correct orientation is shown as cue for this class (Fig. 5.3c), and it was
suggested that the subjects could imagine lifting a dumbell.
(a) Palmar grasp (Class 1).
(b) Pinch (Class 2).
(c) Elbow Flexion (Class 3).
Figure 5.3: Illustration of the movements pertaining to each class (pictures shown in the
visual interface of the BCI system).
Each session of ME comprises of 5 runs of 18 trials (6 for each class) and each session of
MI consists of 6 runs of the same length. Between runs, the subjects could talk or move.
The motor execution session was slightly shorter so as to try to reduce the strain felt by
the subjects during the course of the experiment, especially since the MI task came after
that and is mentally more demanding. Furthermore, results in the literature report slightly
better results for ME tasks when compared to MI, so it was considered to be easier to
31
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
classify and therefore as needing less training data to achieve the same results. This means
that for MI the training was made on 36 trials per class (equivalent to 2.4 minutes of
continuous task and 14 minutes in total considering the breaks between trials) and for ME
the training comprised of 30 trials per class (2 minutes of continuous data, 12 minutes in
total). Such a short training time would allow for a ﬂexible and easily personalized BCI-
based rehabilitation system, where the training program more suitable for each patient
could be rapidly learned and adapted to the patients’ brain signals.
In Fig. 5.4, the timing scheme of the experiment as well as the visual interface accompanying
it are presented. Each trial started with a warning sound and a ﬁxation cross being shown
on the screen, where the subject was supposed to gain his or her focus and mentally prepare
for the task. The ﬁxation cross was then replaced by a visual cue indicating which of the
three motor tasks the subject should perform or imagine.
The subjects were asked to maintain the postures while the corresponding cue was shown
on the screen (4 seconds). After the motor task, the screen became blank and the subject
could rest for 2 seconds. During the experiment, the subjects were asked to rest their right
hand comfortably on the table in front of them and keep all movements to a minimum,
including eye blinks, during the MI and ﬁxation cross parts.
Figure 5.4: Timing scheme and interface appearance of one trial.
5.2.1.2
Online feedback session
In the online session, the setup and timing scheme were similar to the training session, ex-
cept for a vertical bar positioned on the right side of each cue displaying real-time feedback.
The downsampling factor for the classiﬁcation was 8 to be in accordance with the sampling
32
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
frequency; during the ﬁrst second of MI or ME there was no feedback and the data was
concatenated. Once 1 second of EEG corresponding to the motor task was obtained, the
user started receiving feedback: if the window of data was correctly classiﬁed one small
segment of the feedback bar would turn green. The window of data to be classiﬁed would
grow with incoming data until its size corresponded to 2 seconds of data, at which point
it would become a sliding window (the newest samples were incorporated for the classiﬁ-
cation, while the oldest were left out). When a misclassiﬁcation occurred, the green bar
would remain unchanged. This was done to focus on positive feedback and reduce user
frustration.
5.2.2
Data recording
The recordings were done on 14 healthy subjects aged between 20-31 (average 26.3), all
with normal or corrected vision. Only one was left handed.
The recordings were made using the facilities from the Hearing Systems Laboraotry (De-
partment of Electrical Engineering) of the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The
subjects were sitting comfortably in an oﬃce chair distanced approximately one meter
from the computer screen displaying the visual interface, in a sound proof room without
disturbances from the outside.
The data was recorded using the equipment from g.tec medical engineering (listed in Table
5.2), all of which were provided by DTU.
Table 5.2: Equipment used for the EEG data acquisition.
Equipment
Description
g.USBamp ampliﬁer
16 channel biosignal ampliﬁer device with USB interface.

with built-in hardware ﬁlters.
g.GAMMAcap
Electrode cap with 74 available standard positions according to the
international extended 10-20 system.
