the location of the passive electrodes.
As explained in Table 5.2, in this experiment active electrodes were used. This type of elec-
33
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 5. DATA AND EXPERIMENT DESCRIPTION
trodes, made of sintered Ag/AgCl, have an additional pre-ampliﬁer built inside them which
eliminates or greatly reduces the artifacts and recorded noise caused by high impedances
between skin and electrode (namely 50 Hz coupling, background noise, distorted signals
and artifacts resulting from electrode or cable movements) [102]. This means that skin
abrasion becomes unnecessary as a preparatory step for EEG recording when using these
types of electrodes.
Therefore, before the experiment, only the location of the passive
ground and reference electrodes (forehead and left ear lobe, respectively) were prepared for
the recording by the use of an abrasive gel and cleaning with alcohol swipes.
The electrode montage used in the experiment is shown in Fig. 5.5. It was chosen based
on the expected location of MI (the electrodes span the motor cortex, as shown in Fig. 2.1
of chapter 2) and on BCI previous studies involving MI, such as [53, 60, 79].
Figure 5.5: Electrode montage (16 electrodes over the sensorimotor cortex area).
Prior to the start of the experiment, EEG signals obtained from 10 seconds of resting state
from the subject were analyzed. If any anomalies were detected in terms of amplitude or
form, the respective electrode would be checked. This procedure was done in an attempt
to reduce artifacts, since no impedances could be measured with active electrodes.
34
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 6
Signal processing and data analysis
approaches
In this chapter, the EEG signal processing approaches used in the project as well as the
data analysis methods are described. As illustrated in Fig. 3.1, the signal processing part
of a BCI system comprises of several distinct steps: pre-processing to remove noise and
prepare the data, feature extraction to obtain the relevant characteristics of the signal and
ﬁnally classiﬁcation to label the responses. In sections 6.1 to 6.3, we go through each of
these steps individually, describing the selected approaches for each of them. In section
6.4.2, we describe the methods used to analyze the data in both user dependent and semi-
independent situations.
6.1
Pre-processing
The pre-processing stage consisted of band-pass ﬁltering using a 4th order zero-phase distor-
tion Butterworth ﬁlter with cut-of frequencies 7-30 Hz. This interval was chosen because it
allows for the elimination of EOG artifacts, which are known to have predominance in the
lower frequencies, namely in the 0.5-4.0 Hz band [103]. In this fashion we also remove the
physiological delta activity from the EEG signal, but that does not pose a problem since we
are mostly interested in the mu and beta rhythms for MI detection (sometimes also theta).
The upper limit is 30 Hz as it permits reducing the inﬂuence of muscle artifacts, normally
in higher frequencies, and they are not the most signiﬁcant for MI paradigms. An Inﬁnite
Impulse Response (IIR) ﬁlter was chosen because of its eﬃciency and lower computational
load, comparing to a Finite Impulse Response (FIR) ﬁlter; the zero-phase implementation
ensures that no distortion occurs due to the non-linearity of the phase characteristics of an
IIR ﬁlter.
The EEGLAB toolbox for Matlab [104] was used to load and epoch the data.
35
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
6.2
Feature extraction through spatial ﬁltering
In this project, we have developed a sample-wise ACSP using the RLS approach.
The
algorithm therefore allows for:
• Eﬃcient sample-wise increment of the spatial ﬁlter coeﬃcients.
The ﬁlter coeﬃcients can be adapted for each new sample using a method that is
computationally inexpensive and requires small memory space, as there is no need to
store all the data.
• Adaptation of the ﬁlter coeﬃcients to new circumstances or subjects.
The ﬁlter coeﬃcients can adapt to the constant changes in the EEG signals, account-
ing for the non-stationarities in the EEG signal as well as the inter-subject diﬀerences.
We will ﬁrst describe the CSP algorithm, as the adaptive version is based on it.
6.2.1
Common Spatial Patterns
The purpose of the CSP ﬁlter is to identify the source locations of neuro electric signals
while distinguishing between two classes, or populations (e.g. MI of left and right hand).
Let Xj ∈RCn×N be the jth EEG trial, where N is the number of samples per trial and Cn
is the number of EEG channels. Then, we can represent the ﬁltered trial Zj,CSP ∈RCn×N
as:
Zj,CSP = W Xj,
(6.1)
where W ∈RCn×Cn is the matrix parameterizing the signal decomposition, which projects
the recorded EEG signal from sensor to source space. Here, we denote each column of W
as a spatial ﬁlter, and each column of W−1 as a spatial pattern.
Since Xj has been high pass ﬁltered in the pre-processing stage, it is now a zero-mean signal
i.e. it is centered [105]. Thus, we can only rely on the variance to distinguish between
classes. The basic principle behind the CSP algorithm is to maximize the variance of one
class while maximizing it for the other. For a band-pass ﬁltered signal, the variance equals
the band-power and therefore this algorithm is used to eﬀectively discriminate between
distinct mental states described by ERD/ERS eﬀects [106].
The classical method [105] involves the common diagonalization of the covariance matrices
of both classes. Then we can deﬁne the normalized covariance matrix (i.e. correlation
matrix) for class k as:
Ck = 1
Tn
Tn
X
j=1
Xj(k)XT
j(k)
trace(Xj(k)XT
j(k)),
(6.2)
36
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
where Tn is the number of trials, Xj(k) is the jth trial belonging to class k ∈[1, K] and
K = 2 for the binary classiﬁcation case, which we will use, for simplicity, to explain the
algorithm.
The CSP analysis is then made by the common diagonalization of the two covariance
matrices:
WC1WT = Λ1,
WC2WT = Λ2,
(6.3)
where Λk is the diagonal matrix of eigenvalues and W are the eigenvectors. The scaling
of W is determined so that Λ1 + Λ2 = I. This can be done by solving the Generalized
Eigenvalue Decomposition (GED) problem:
C1wi = λiC2wi,
(6.4)
where λi = wiCkwT
i is the ith eigenvalue in Λk and wi are the column vectors of W i.e.
the generalized eigenvectors.
A detailed solution for this problem is as follows:
• Computing the composite spatial covariance Ccp = C1 + C2;
• Factorizing Ccp: Ccp = UcΛcUT
c , where Uc is the matrix of eigenvectors;
• Equalizing the variance in the space generated by Uc by performing a whitening
transformation: P =
q
1
Λc UT
c ;
• Transforming C1 and C2: Sk = PCkPT ;
Note: S1 and S2 share common eigenvectors B and, as seen before, Λ1 + Λ2 = I.
Thus, the eigenvector with largest eigenvalue for class 1 has the smallest eigenvector
for class 2, and vice-versa. Therefore, taking the m ﬁrst and last eigenvectors of B
and using them to project the EEG data provides the most discriminative features to
distinguish between class 1 and 2, in the least squares sense [105].
The value of m generally varies between 1 and 3 - too large a value could result in
overﬁtting of the classiﬁer parameters as well as a more complex system and, in some
cases, a small value for m can be insuﬃcient to discriminate the two classes.
• Obtaining the projection matrix W = (BT P)T .
This was the basic CSP algorithm implemented in this project.
Blankertz and colleagues [106] have described the CSP with a discriminative view, which
was useful to derive the adaptive algorithm. Considering
Cd = C1 −C2
and
Cc = C1 + C2
(6.5)
37
==PAGE:52==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
as the discriminative activity (Cd), i.e. the band-power modulation between the two classes,
and the common activity (Cc). Then, the solution to the following maximization problem
can be achieved by solving the same GED problem:
argmax
W
WT CdW
WT CcW.
(6.6)
The solution to the maximization problem in Eq. (6.6) is also referred to as the Rayleight
coeﬃcient. It maximizes the discriminative activity between the conditions while minimiz-
ing the common activity, which we are not interested in.
6.2.2
Multiclass approach
As previously stated, a multiclass approach to the CSP must be deﬁned so that the al-
gorithm can be used to distinguish between more than two classes. The One-Versus-All
(OVA), also commonly called One-Versus-Rest (OVR) was the strategy implemented in
this project, as it is one of the most widely used and has proven to show superior results
when comparing with others like Divide-And-Conquer and similar results with some other
strategies like the Pair-Wise approach [101, 107].
The OVA strategy implies that one projection matrix of CSP weights is obtained for each
class, by maximizing its variance in relation to the sum of the other classes.
6.2.3
Regularized Common Spatial Patterns
Since one of the goals of the project is to determine the feasibility of a system trained on
a small amount of data, a regularized version of the CSP algorithm was used to compute
the spatial ﬁlters in that situation (i.e. in the second dataset, which was recorded during
this project). Several methods were considered based on the paper by Lotte and Guan [87]
and the regularized CSP with Diagonal Loading (DLCSP) was chosen among them. One
of the main advantages of this method is that the regularizing parameter does not need to
be estimated through cross-validation, being instead automatically obtained using Ledoit
and Wolf’s method [108] - its implementation is therefore faster.
Furthermore, despite
the fact that it did not produce signiﬁcant improvements in the experiment in [87], it has
shown to be signiﬁcantly more eﬃcient than CSP in other projects (in [88] for example)
and particularly for small training sizes, which is precisely the situation we have.
This approach consists of shrinking the covariance matrix towards the identity matrix using
the regularization parameter γ ∈[0, 1]. Its regularized estimate eCk is then computed as:
eCk = (1 −γ) ˆCk + γI,
(6.7)
where I is the identity matrix and ˆCk is the class covariance matrix estimate computed as
in Eq. (6.2).
38
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
To implement the DLCSP algorithm, the RCSP toolbox for Matlab created by the authors
of [87] was used, and was also an inspiration for the implementation of our own CSP
algorithm.
6.2.4
Adaptive Common Spatial Patterns (ACSP) algorithm
This adaptive version of the CSP algorithm is based on the RLS method for generalized
eigendecomposition [109] and was also inspired by the axDAWN algorithm [98].
In this section the algorithm is introduced for the binary case for simpliﬁcation purposes;
it is implemented in the OVA multiclass approach explained in section 6.2.2.
A training set is used to initialize the CSP matrix, which is computed using the method
explained in section 6.2.1. In practice, this calibration session can be reduced or the weights
can originate from diﬀerent subjects, since the adaptation should ensure that the ﬁlter
converges to the optimal solution for each subject.
As we have seen, the CSP problem can be described in a discriminative fashion, where it
implies solving the GED problem described in Eq. (6.6). We can expand it and conclude
that:

A few comments must be made regarding Eq. (6.11) and the components needed to compute

(a) Iterative computation of class covariance matrices:
In this project, in the context of BCI for stroke rehabilitation, we considered it was
not of the highest importance to create an asynchronous, self-paced BCI system. This
results in an advantage for the development of the adaptive CSP, speciﬁcally in this
step, because we always know the true label of each sample, i.e., we always know the

information from the new data that needs to be stored.
(b) Iterative computation of the inverse of common activity covariance matrix:
Inverting Cc(n) to compute ˆw1(n) for every iteration in Eq. (6.11) would imply a very
high of computational eﬀort which is not feasible for an online application. Therefore,
as in [98, 109], the Sherman-Morrison-Woodbury formula for the iterative update of
matrix inversion [110] is used:

6.2.5
Features
As features, we have taken the logarithm of the variance of the spatial ﬁltered signal,
which is the most common feature type used with the CSP algorithm.
Other features
were investigated, such as the bandpower of the mu and beta rhythms, but preliminary
results indicated an increase in computational time that was not satisfactory followed by
an increase in classiﬁcation accuracy and therefore such features were not used. Therefore,
each trial was characterized by 12 features (four CSP vectors per class k, two maximizing
the variance for class k and the other maximizing the variance for the sum of the remaining
classes). Feature fi ∈R2m, corresponding to the CSP channel i, can then be deﬁned as:
42
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
fi = log

var(zmj)
,

where var stands for variance, zi indicates channel i of the projected trial and m indicates
the number of pairs of CSP ﬁlters taken from each end of the CSP matrix that are used for
the classiﬁcation.
6.3
Classiﬁcation
Several classiﬁcation methods were taken into consideration when developing the project,
namely the SVM, Linear and Quadratic Discriminant Analysis (LDA and QDA, respec-
tively) and Naive Bayesian classiﬁers, since these are some of the most commonly used in
current BCI paradigms (refer to Tables 4.1 and 4.2). From these options, SVM is generally
considered to yield the best results, however it also involves the longest computational time
and highest memory consumption [98]. On the other hand, LDA and QDA are amongst
the simplest possible classiﬁers, which represents an advantage when considering a real-time
feedback system as the one that was implemented in this project. LDA is a very simple
method requiring low computational eﬀort which often performs as good as, or even better
than, some of the most complex methods: some studies using MI-based BCI systems sug-
gest that SVM and LDA can show similar classiﬁcation results [60, 111]. Furthermore, a
study by Lotte and colleagues [112] conﬁrmed LDA as one of the most eﬃcient classiﬁers
in BCI systems.
However, LDA also has some limitations: the assumption that the data follows a normal
distribution can be a limitation, the computation of the per-class covariance matrix is
particularly sensitive to outliers and there are cases when the classes cannot be separated
by linear boundaries [113, 114]. There are various methods available to, to some extent,
overcome this weakness of the LDA classiﬁer. Here we have chosen to use the regularized
version of a Discriminant Analysis (DA) classiﬁer described by Friedman [115] which is
implemented in the Biosig toolbox [116].
Ideally, an adaptive classiﬁer should be used, for the same reasons that an adaptive spatial
ﬁlter is preferred over a stationary one, however due to time limitations such an adaptive
version was not implemented.
We begin by introducing the concept of DA and subsequently its regularized version.
6.3.1
Discriminant Analysis
The traditional method of using DA as a classiﬁer was introduced by R. Fisher and is
known as Fisher’s LDA (FLDA) [117, 118].
43
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
Let us consider we have K diﬀerent populations belonging to K diﬀerent classes which
are assumed to have a normal distribution with mean vector µk and covariance matrix Σk
(k ∈[1, K]). From these populations, we have randomly selected N observations x, such
that there are x1,1, ..., x1,N1 samples from class 1, x2,1, ..., x2,N2 samples from class 2, and
so forth, where N = N1 + N2 + ... + NK. Assuming that the sample labels are known, the
purpose of LDA (as that of any classiﬁer) is to use these observations as training samples
to establish a classiﬁcation rule. In the 2-class case (K = 2), this is done by calculating
a projection vector v that maximizes the diﬀerence between the projected means while
minimizing the within-class variance. Figure 6.1 provides a visualization of the method for
a 2D feature space.
Figure 6.1: Schematic representation of class separation done by LDA. Observations are
depicted from 2 populations (blue and green). LDA projects the data in a direction such
that the distance between the projected means (µ1 and µ2) is maximized at the same time
that the within-class variances (s1 and s2) are minimized. Figure modiﬁed from [119]
This classiﬁcation rule can be generalized for any value of K: LDA classiﬁes the sample xk,i
to the population ˆk which minimizes the quantity (xk,i −µˆk)T Σ−1
k (xk,i −µˆk). Often there
is knowledge about the prior probabilities of each class (proportion of each population in
the N observations), which we denote as πk, such that π1 + π2 + ... + πK = 1. LDA then
maximizes the posterior probability that xk,i belongs to a particular class, which results in
the classiﬁcation rule:
dˆk(xk,i) = min
k∈[1,K] dk(xk,i),
(6.17)
where
dk(xk,i) = (xk,i −µˆk)T Σ−1
k (xk,i −µˆk) + ln|Σk| −2lnπk
(6.18)
44
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
is the discriminant score for class k. The classiﬁcation rule in Eqs. (6.17 and 6.18) results
in QDA, since the boundaries separating the observations from each class are quadratic.
In the case where all the covariance matrices are assumed to be identical we then have the
special case of linear boundaries, which results in the well known LDA. Both methods are
assumed to work well when the class conditional densities are close to normal and good
estimates can be obtained for the mean vectors µk and covariance matrices Σk. In general,
QDA seems to require more data and be more sensitive to violations of these assumptions,
making LDA a more generally reliable classiﬁer.
In practice, the parameters associated with the class densities are estimated from the sam-
ples:

6.3.1.1
Friedman’s Regularized Discriminant Analysis
It is known that the computations in Eq. (6.19) result in biased estimates of the eigenvalues
[115]. Furthermore, in problems where the number of parameters to be estimated exceeds
the number of observations, the parameter estimate becomes unstable and leads to high
variance. Methods of regularization attempt to improve the estimates by leading them
away from the sample-based values towards more “physically plausible” ones [115].
Friedman’s approach introduces two parameters to regularize the sample covariance matrix
estimates [115]. The ﬁrst, η ∈[0, 1] and controls the degree of shrinkage of the individual
class covariance matrix estimates towards their average/pooled estimate. This parameter
gives the algorithm the ﬂexibility to vary between LDA (η = 1) and QDA (η = 0), according
to the more appropriate case for each problem.
The second regularization parameter,
γ ∈[0, 1], is identical to the one used for the regularization of the CSP algorithm: it
controls shrinkage towards a multiple of the identity matrix, which reduces the bias in the
eigenvalue estimates. The regularized sample class covariance matrix is then computed as:
ˆΣk(η, γ) = (1 −γ) ˆΣk(η) + γ
ptrace[ ˆΣk(η)]I,
(6.20)
where I stands for the identity matrix, p for the dimension of the measurement space and
ˆΣk(η) is computed as:
ˆΣk(η) = Sk(η)
Nk(η),
(6.21)
with
Sk(η) = (1 −η)Sk + ηS,
Nk(η) = (1 −η)Nk + ηN,
(6.22)
where S =
K
X
k=1
Sk and Sk is given in Eq. (6.19).
45
==PAGE:60==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
The two regularization parameters are then chosen to jointly minimize the misclassiﬁcation
risk and produce unbiased estimates. This approach is named Regularized Discriminant
Analysis (RDA) and oﬀers a certain variety of regularization options.
In practice, the
covariance-matrix mixing parameter η and eigenvalue shrinkage paramater γ need to be
estimated based on a training set.
The method used in this project to ﬁnd the optimal regularization parameters was by
doing a grid-search during L-fold cross-validation, which is a common approach, albeit
computationally demanding. For each Lth cross-validation fold, the training set is divided
into Q sub-folds, of which Q −1 subfolds are used as a training where all combinations of
η and γ in the grid search are used to train a classiﬁer, which is tested on the Qth sub-
fold. for each combination of parameters the accuracy is averaged over all Q sub-folds and
the best set is chosen. It is then tested on the Lth testing set to produce unbiased ﬁnal
cross-validation results. In our approach, L and Q were set to 10.
6.4
Data analysis methods
In this section, we explain the structure of the data analysis presented in the results and
the methods used to evaluate the algorithms.
6.4.1
Structure of the data analysis
As explained in Chapter 5, the adaptive algorithm is evaluated on two distinct datasets.
We begin testing it on the BCI competition data as a proof of concept and to be able
to compare the results with the state of the art algorithm FBCSP. The structure of the
analysis is as follows:
1. Generic user dependent strategy: one CSP ﬁlter and RDA classiﬁer are trained
for each subject using all the data from the calibration session and tested on the
evaluation data. This strategy is expected to result in better classiﬁcation but has
the drawback that training sessions without feedback are undesirable.
2. Investigation of a semi user independent strategy: we opt for relying on shorter
calibration sessions to initialize the feature extraction and classiﬁcation parameters
instead of totally eliminating them (and initializing the parameters with data from
a database, for instance). The reason for this is that an ideal BCI system for the
rehabilitation of stroke patients can be customized to the speciﬁc needs of the patients;
having a short calibration session allows for such an individualization of the system,
where the movements more suitable for the recovery of each patient can be easily
learned. Here there is a trade-oﬀbetween early feedback and a personalized system.
For this purpose, diﬀerent sizes of the training data are used to calibrate the param-
eters and tested on the evaluation set. The classiﬁcation performances are used to
assess the feasibility of using smaller training sets.
46
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
After testing the algorithm in the BCI competition data it is evaluated on our dataset.
Our data is composed of two parts: MI and ME. Its analysis is therefore also divided into
two distinct parts, where for each motor task the cross-validation and online performances
are presented. Since the size of the calibration session is small, the performance of the
normal CSP is compared to that its regularized version described in section 6.2.3. The
scalp topographies of the spatial ﬁlters are analyzed, as well as its convergence and the
performance over time.
6.4.2
Performance evaluation methods
The kappa value, also known as Cohen’s kappa coeﬃcient, was introduced in 1960 by
Cohen [120] as a statistical measure of agreement between two categories (in this case real
and predicted class labels) that incorporates information about the chance level, thereby
showing a more unbiased estimate of performance. Kappa is deﬁned as:
k = po −pe
1 −pe
,
(6.23)
where po is the observed agreement between raters (commonly known as accuracy) and pe
is the chance level. When there is perfect agreement the kappa value is equal to 1; when the
classiﬁed accuracy is equal to chance level, according to the number of classes, the kappa
value is 0. There are diﬀerent ways to interpret the kappa values; a commonly used one is
given by Altman, 1991 [121]:
Table 6.1: Possible interpretation of the kappa value according to Altman [121].
Kappa value
Level of agreement
≤0.20
Poor
[0.21, 0.40]
Fair
[0.41, 0.60]
Moderate
[0.61, 0.80]
Good
[0.81, 1.00]
Very good
Despite the fact that the kappa value is a more “real” measure of performance, most results
in the literature are shown in terms of accuracy due to its more natural interpretation. Nev-
ertheless, our results will be presented in terms of kappa value, both due to its advantages
and because it was the evaluation parameter used in the BCI competition IV. Speciﬁcally,
the kappa value is obtained through the computation of confusion matrices constructed for
each sample, given a continuous classiﬁcation output. The average value of kappa over the
time-course of a trial is then obtained, and the maximum value reached is used to compare
the results of the competition [122].
47
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 6. SIGNAL PROCESSING AND DATA ANALYSIS APPROACHES
6.4.3
Signiﬁcance analysis: one-sided paired t-test
A one-sided paired t-test was used to test whether or not the diﬀerence in the performance
of two diﬀerent algorithms is signiﬁcant, under the assumption that the samples are drawn
from two normal distributions. The null hypothesis implicates that the diﬀerence between
the sample means is d0 and the alternative hypothesis that the diﬀerence between the means
is larger than d0 [123]. to is obtained as:
to = ¯
aD −d0
sD
√
Ns
,
(6.24)
where ¯
aD represents the mean of the diﬀerences between the two samples, sD the standard
deviation of the diﬀerences and Ns is the sample size. The null hypothesis is rejected with
α conﬁdence level if to > t1−α for Ns −1 degrees of freedom.
48
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 7
Results
In this chapter, the results for both datasets are presented. The classiﬁcation performances
and scalp topographies are analyzed and compared for both the adaptive and non-adaptive
feature extraction algorithms.
7.1
4-class MI of diﬀerent limbs: BCI competition IV dataset
2a
The analysis of this 4-class dataset is divided in two distinct parts: section 7.1.1 presents
the results obtained following the rules of the competition, as provided in section 5.1.3, and
compares the classiﬁcation performance of the developed adaptive CSP with the winners
of the BCI competition for this dataset, Ang et al. [101]; section 7.1.2 shows the results
obtained using the strategy implemented in an eﬀort to achieve user independence by
reducing the size of the training session.

7.1.1.2
Convergence analysis on unseen evaluation data
A simulation of an online session was made using the methods described in 5.1.4.
An
investigation of the convergence of the adaptive ﬁlter was made prior to the analysis of
the classiﬁcation performances, as the results from the classiﬁcation from the adaptive
algorithm should only be taken into consideration after the algorithm has converged (they
have no real meaning before that).
Rao and Principe [109] have proven that the RLS type algorithm for GED converges to the
real solution and have shown that the number iterations necessary for convergence depends
on the dimension of the data. In Fig. 7.1, the estimation of the generalized eigenvalues
for each binary case over the course of the online simulation session is shown for subject
1. As explained in section 6.2.4, this estimate corresponds to the Rayleigh coeﬃcient in
Eq. (6.8). The estimates corresponding to the two ﬁrst and last generalized eigenvectors
is plotted, as those were the ones used to project the EEG data and discriminate between
the classes.

From the convergence plot of Fig. 7.1, we can conclude that the algorithm takes approxi-
mately 10000 samples per class to converge (i.e. approximately four times that in total).
The plots of Fig. 7.1 also indicate that there could be some issues with the convergence
of the algorithm: while some of the eigenvectors seem to be properly converging (and also
doing it earlier), others take more iterations to stabilize, if they stabilize at all. From subject
1, one of the most critical examples are the ﬁrst and second generalized eigenvalues of class
4, which do not seem to have achieved their ﬁnal value even after 35000 iterations. On the
other hand, most of the third and fourth GE are stabilizing. One possible explanation for
the problems in the convergence can be that the update of the estimates is not continuous for
each class, as happens in the original formulation of the algorithm [109] and in the axDAWN
[98]. This happens because the numerator of the Rayleigh coeﬃcient in Eq. (6.8) for class
k is only updated when the current trial belongs to class k, which is one out of 4 trials. To
investigate whether the problems in convergence could originate from the discontinuities
in the data originated from having diﬀerent classes, we display the generalized eigenvalue
estimates over the iterations only for class 4, with the time stamps of the trial beginnings
marked.

iterations.

Hz, this means we can expect acceptable estimates of the CSP weights after 12 trials per
class. In the cases where the values do not seem to totally stabilize, we can look at the
scalp topographies to investigate whether the algorithm has converged to a true solution
or diverged into something physiologically non-signiﬁcant.

The patterns of subject 1 can also be observed for the other subjects. We can then gener-

who was the subject with the lowest classiﬁcation performance).

We can see that in the case of the good subjects, such as subject 1, the scalp topographies
match clearly the physiological areas of sensorimotor cortex activation for classes one to
three (contra-lateral activation for the hands and central for the feet. Class four (tongue
movement) can involve activation of both cerebral hemispheres and shows therefore less
well-deﬁned activation sites in the topographical maps. A comparison of the spatial ﬁlters
before and after adaptation to the new data shows very little diﬀerences, indicating that:
(a) the adaptive algorithm is converging to the right solution and (b) in this case, large
increases in accuracy are probably not to be expected.
For subjects performing worse, like subjects 9 and 6, the scalp topographies do not always
57
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS
show what was to be expected. Improvements caused by the adaptive algorithm can be seen
in some of the weight vectors (the ﬁrst vector of the class 1 CSP matrix from subject 9 and
6, perhaps also the weight vectors of class 3 from subject 6). As the original topographies
from the training session start to be less signiﬁcant, so do the ones resulting from the
adaptation process.
Similar plots can be found in Appendix A for other examples of the eﬀect of the adaptive
process.

results and the ﬁxed CSP are given for all the data.

As expected, the classiﬁcation performance was consistently lower in the unseen evaluation
data with regards to the cross-validation results. Comparing the algorithm’s performances,
the FBCSP performed better on average, followed by the ACSP. A one-sided paired t-test
was carried out to investigate whether the diﬀerence in classiﬁcation performance between
the FBCSP and ACSP algorithms was signiﬁcant. This was done using Eq. (6.24) and
58
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS
setting d0 to zero, i.e., the null hypothesis is that the diﬀerence between the two algorithms
is zero. The p-value obtained at a conﬁdence level of α = 0.05 was 0.141, which is not
enough to reject the null hypothesis; therefore, we cannot say that the FBCSP performed
signiﬁcantly better than the ACSP algorithm for a conﬁdence level of α = 0.05. However,
the ﬁrst still outperformed the latter in in all subjects except three (subjects 1, 6 and 9).
Furthermore, in some subjects (2,7 and 8) the ACSP performed undesirably worse than the
ﬁxed CSP, which also happened with the FBCSP for two subjects (1 and 8).
A similar paired t-test revealed that there is no signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the ﬁxed CSP
and the ACSP (p = 0.294), but the FBCSP performed signiﬁcantly better than the simple
CSP (p = 0.008).

diﬀerent number of trials of the calibration session. The number of trials per class was made
to vary from 15 to 65 and the resulting kappa values corresponding to the classiﬁcation
performances on the evaluation dataset were calculated for each. The trials chosen for each
class were taken randomly.The result is presented in Fig. 7.13.

It is clear that the performance of the ﬁxed algorithm decreases signiﬁcantly in accordance

ﬁnal one already one from a very small training size. While the diﬀerence in performance
kappa for a training size of 72 is only 0.021, for a training size of 15 the diﬀerence is 0.188,
and 0.115 for a size of 35 trials per class. This represents a 1.69-fold increase for the ﬁrst
and 1.28-fold increase for the latter, which is a similar training size than the one chosen for
our experiment.
7.2
3-class MI of single limb
Here we analyze the results obtained for the MI tasks in the experiment executed during

7.2.1
Training session
In Table 7.6, the 10 × 10 - fold cross-validation performances for our training set of 36
trials per class using the CSP ﬁlter and RDA is given as average kappa value. The training
performances using the regularized version of the CSP, Diagonal Loading Common Spatial
Patterns (DLCSP) entailed similar results and is therefore not shown here.
Clearly the small training size and the complexity of the task have resulted in a much poorer
performance of the CSP algorithm compared to the previous dataset. A kappa value of
0.255 corresponds to an accuracy of 50% in a 3-class problem and a training accuracy of at
least 60% (corresponding to a kappa value of 0.4) is to be expected for a 3-class problem
with diﬀerent motor tasks performed by the same limb, according to some results in the
literature [59, 60]. However, the system implemented for training is indeed a very simple
one and the small training size reduces its performance even more. Nevertheless, the results

7.2.2
Convergence analysis of data from the online session
Before the beginning of the online session for all the subjects, data was acquired in the
same situation and analyzed to assess when the adaptive ﬁlter can be considered to have
adapted. Similar plots to the ones shown in Fig. 7.1 were obtained and it was determined
that convergence should occur around 7500 samples which, with a sampling frequency of 256
Hz, a downsampling factor of 2 and 3.5 seconds of adaptation per trial, indicates that around
17 trials per class are needed for convergence of the algorithm. This number is somewhat
higher than expected and entails a disadvantage of the method, since the feedback is not
“real” until the ﬁlter has converged. Further comments on ﬁlter convergence are made in
section 8.6.

online session and therefore his entry is ommited. A comparison between the simple CSP
and its regularized version DLCSP is made, to asses whether or not the use of regularization
was necessary for this problem.

the adaptive algorithm, resulting in an increase in mean kappa value of 0.046, even though

5 × 10−5) and its regularized version (p < 10−5). Furthermore, the use of the adaptive
algorithm has, compared to the cross-validation results, increased the performance kappa
values to be more in accordance with the literature: Yong et al. [60] achieved a kappa
value of 0.41 on a similar MI 3-class problem (rest against elbow and grasp movements)
and Jochumsen and colleagues [59] achieved k = 0.445.

number of trials represented is 500.

The rows
correspond to the true classes and the columns to the classes that were predicted by the
algorithm.
Figure 7.15 allows us to comment on the discriminability between the three diﬀerent classes.
The right-most column represents the percentage of the trials belonging to class k that were
correctly classiﬁed, and the bottom row represents the percentage of trials predicted as class
k that actually belong to it. The value on the right-bottom of the matrix represents the
overall percentage of correctly classiﬁed trials (corresponding to the ﬁnal kappa value). The
asymmetry of the confusion matrix suggests a certain bias of the classiﬁer towards the third
class, elbow ﬂexion: a high percentage of trials were misclassiﬁed as class 3 (21.% of the
overall trials).

Analyzing the resulting topographies we can conclude that, as expected, distinguishing the
sources of a three-class problem concerning movements from the same limb is a much harder
task for the CSP algorithm compared to discriminating between movements from diﬀerent
limbs. The physiological sources of electrical activity in the sensorimotor cortex involved
in each of the three diﬀerent motor tasks are much closer to each other, thereby rendering
the task of separating it more complicated, particularly when using EEG due to its poor
spatial resolution, and even more so when a relatively small number of electrodes is used,
as was the case in this project.
67
==PAGE:82==
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS
Nevertheless, in some cases the CSP resultig from the calibration session (e.g. subjects 1
and 9) seems to have failed to identify even the general source of activity (in this case the
left hemisphere of the brain). This may implicate that the regularization was not enough
to construct physiologically signiﬁcant spatial ﬁlters from so little training data. In some
cases, after adaptation the sources’ locations become more clear (for example for the ﬁrst
CSP vector of class 1 from subject 1, or, very loosely, for the second CSP vectors for all
classes from subject 9). In other cases, however, the adaptive ﬁlter does not seem to lead
to more physiologically meaningful patterns.
An interesting case to point out is that of subject 9, who had one of the worst training
results (kappa value of 0.045) but the best online result (kappa value of 0.705). Indeed,
the CSP ﬁlters resulting from the calibration session do not seem to carry any substantial
information on source location; after adaptation, however, it is possible to identify some of
the sources, mainly through the second weight vectors. Nevertheless, the ﬁrst vectors seem
to be fairly random.

71
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 8
Discussion
In this chapter, we discuss the results presented in chapter 7 and compare them to some
of the state of the art literature presented in Chapter 4. The advantages and limitations
of the chosen approach are examined and considerations are made about relevant future
work.
8.1
ACSP performance in long versus short training sessions
The results obtained in section 7.1.2, where the training size was varied for the BCI com-
petition data, allow us to conclude that the use of the adaptive CSP is only advantageous
when the training size is small. The results indicated that, from very short training sessions
(15 trials per class), the adaptive CSP enables classiﬁcation performances comparable to
the ones from the ﬁxed CSP when the training size is much larger, such as 72 trials per
class.
This was also conﬁrmed by comparing the results obtained both from the 4-class data,
where a long training session of 72 trials per class was available to train the CSP, and from
the 3-class data, where only 36 trials were available. In the ﬁrst case, the adaptive ﬁlter
resulted in an increase in performance kappa of 0.021, which was not signiﬁcant. However,
for both MI and ME in the second dataset, the increase in classiﬁcation kappa value was
4-fold and 1.5-fold, respectively, which are signiﬁcantly superior results.
It is known that the computation of the CSP ﬁlter is a particularly data-hungry procedure:
it requires large amounts of data to avoid overﬁtting and to provide a robust estimate.
In this sense, we can assume that the ﬁlters obtained from the training data on the 3-
class dataset were not the best solution and that there is “room for improvement”.
In
this case, we can say that the adaptive procedure has a bigger overall eﬀect and that the
inclusion of new data for the adaptation of the ﬁlter coeﬃcients leads to a more appropriate
solution. On the other hand, when the initial weights were already considered to be the
best solution, since the CSP was computed on the ideal situation of having a large amount
of data, the adaptation lead to little improvement. In this case, the winning algorithm
72
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 8. DISCUSSION
of the BCI competition, FBCSP, performed better than both adaptive and non-adaptive
CSP implemented in this project.
It was to be expected that it would outperform the
simple CSP algorithm and, since the situation was such that the adaptive ﬁlter brought
little improvement, it is no surprise that the FBCSP outperformed both. Nevertheless,
our adaptive algorithm would achieve a second place in the competition, tied with Liu
Guangquan and colleagues who placed second in the actual competition.
Similar conclusions to the above can be drawn from comparing the scalp topographies
before and after adaptation. In the cases where the adaptive ﬁlter results in small or no
increases in performance, the topography plots of the activation areas remained similar
before and after, meaning that the ﬁlter coeﬃcients suﬀered little changes. In the cases
where the adaptation lead to higher increases in performance, it is possible to identify bigger
changes in the scalp topographies which seem to locate the sources at more physiologically
signiﬁcant places.
In conclusion, the usage of such an adaptive CSP is only beneﬁcial for smaller amounts of
training data. This represents a potential step towards an individualized BCI system for
stroke rehabilitation.
8.2
Regularized versus non-regularized CSP
The results obtained from the DLCSP - the regularized version of the spatial ﬁlter chosen
in this project - were somewhat surprising.
In the MI case, it lead to superior results
(although not signiﬁcantly so) but, in the ME task, no improvements were seen from using
the regularized version. These results do not seem to be in accordance with the literature
(for example in the work by Handiru and colleagues, where a similar shrinkage regularization
of the CSP resulted in an increase of about 8% in accuracy). Furthermore, when only the
ﬁxed CSP ﬁlter was used, the regularized resulted in a signiﬁcant decrease in accuracy.
These results are surprising since it would be expected that, if the eﬀect of regularization
was not necessary to increase the performance, the automatic procedure using Ledoit and
Wolf’s method would be able to set the regularization parameter γ to zero, which didn’t
happen. However, the results indicate that initial worse CSP weights are not problem for
the adaptive CSP, since it either converges to the exact same solution (as in the case of
ME) or a slightly better one (as in the case of MI).
8.3
MI and ME
In Table 8.1, the best results obtained in our 3-class dataset for both MI and ME are
summarized. The best results were obtained using the adaptive CSP version implemented
in this project.
73
==PAGE:88==
CHAPTER 8. DISCUSSION
Table 8.1: Summary of best training cross-validation (CV) and online evaluation perfor-
mances in terms of kappa value for MI and ME in our dataset.
MI eee
ME eee
CV
Online
CV
Online
Average kappa
0.255 ± 0.045
0.474
0.197 ± 0.089
0.420
Overall, both for training and online results, the performance of the MI tasks was superior
to that of the ME (maximum kappa of 0.474 versus 0.412), although not signiﬁcantly
(p = 0.095). Such a reduction in performance does not seem to be justiﬁable by a diﬀerence
of 6 trials per class in the training size. A potential explanation could be the presence of
more artifacts due to the movements, although the low-pass ﬁlter at 30 Hz should eliminate
the muscle artifacts. The results are surprising since some literature indicates slightly better
performances for ME [124]. We can only conclude that further experiments must be made
into this subject, perhaps varying the training size to determine if the problem has arisen
from that.
Additionally, the use of electromyography can be helpful in tasks involving
movement execution and could potentially lead to better performances.
8.4
Class separability
The analysis of the confusion matrices and scalp topographies for the 3-class dataset allows
us to draw some conclusions about the class separability.
For the MI case, class 1 and 2 were the easiest to distinguish between each other. In general,
these classes tended to generate spatial ﬁlters with a more localized electrical source, which
could be an explanation as to why they were easier to discriminate. On the other hand,
the classiﬁer seemed biased towards elbow ﬂexion, in the sense that a high percentage of
trials were misclassiﬁed as class 3. An analysis of the CSP spatial topographies reveals
that class 3 often generates less localized ﬁlters, which could implicate that samples where
the source is less clear end up being classiﬁed as belonging to class 3. The same eﬀect was
noted for ME, although not as strongly. A potential explanation is that elbow ﬂexion, when
combined with the action “lifting a dumbbell”, as was suggested to the subjects, involves
not only the arm muscles but also the ones in the hand. Therefore, the active regions in
the sensorimotor are larger and more spread out. A possible way to tackle this problem
would be to introduce a fourth class, corresponding to a rest state. For the purpose of a
practical application, such a class should be considered as well. Finally, for ME class 1 and
2 were the hardest to separate, which is, again, in contradiction to the results from the MI
data.
74
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 8. DISCUSSION
8.5
Implications for clinical applications
As stated by Jochumsen et al. [124], the performance threshold from which the system
performance is able to induce neuroplasticity in the BCI user is not known. Accuracies
of around 60-70% on a two class problem (discriminating between rest and MI), which
correspond to kappa values of 0.2-0.4, were enough to signiﬁcantly increase the cortical
projections related to the appointed muscles when combined with FES. In this project,
average maximum kappa values of 0.472 were achieved for MI, which implicates the imple-
mented paradigm could be of value in a real-life BCI application for neuro-rehabilitation.
It is important to consider, however, that not all subjects reached this level of accuracy -
this may imply that a screening prior to the usage of such a BCI system should be made
on the patients, to assert whether or not they are MI-able (as stated in section 3.3.1.1, it
is estimated that around 13-20% of BCI users could be unable to perform MI).
Furthermore, for clinical applications, it could be more relevant to consider accuracies
instead of kappa values: for instance, for a 3-class problem a kappa value of 0.25 (which is
within the interval which has shown to induce neuroplasticity) corresponds to an accuracy of
50%; but a system that misclassiﬁes half the times can be frustrating for the user. Therefore,
while the kappa value is better to compare algorithms between diﬀerent paradigms because
it is unbiased towards the number of classes, for a clinical application we must ensure that
the frustration levels of the user are reduced, otherwise the patients may not have interest
in using such a system. Such frustration levels were clearly seen throughout the course
of the experiments made during this project, particularly when the subjects’ intent was
being classiﬁed by the ﬁxed spatial ﬁlter - sometimes, the users would even stop trying.
Therefore, it could be relevant to investigate which are the accuracy threshold(s) which
keep a subject from being frustrated.
Nevertheless, we have in this project proved the feasibility of a 3-class problem which could
be used for stroke rehabilitation. Moreover, the short training session (around 15 minutes of
recording without feedback) indicate that it is possible to have a personalized rehabilitation
system. The more relevant movements for the rehabilitation of each individual patient could
be determined by the medical doctor or nurses, and the system could be rapidly trained
and posteriorly adapted to the patient’s EEG signals through the adaptive ﬁlter.
8.6
Limitations and future work
Despite the encouraging results, the developed system still presents some limitations which
prevent it from being used, as it is, in practical applications. One of such limitations is
the number and type of electrodes used. Sixteen electrodes is a relatively small number for
BCI applications, as some are known to use 60 or more, but, regardless, it is impractical
for an everyday use. It takes some time to set up and does not allow the user to move
freely, being instead connected to the ampliﬁer while using the equipment. Furthermore,
the use of gel to reduce the scalp impedances means the subjects must wash their hair after
each session, which can be bothersome. As such, a reduction in the number of channels
75
==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER 8. DISCUSSION
is necessary or, ideally, a portable wireless system with dry electrodes such as the Emotiv
headset, for example, should be used. Performances by the latter are, unfortunately, still
not satisfactory, but signiﬁcant achievements have been made on the channel reduction side,
such as the work by Jochumsen and colleagues [124]. For this system to be implemented,
similar strategies for channel reduction should be investigated.
Another limitation of the present system is the fact that it is a supervised method. While
this is not an impeding factor for its usable in a rehabilitation setting, unsupervised meth-
ods do make the system more interesting and challenging for the user. Furthermore, it
allows the user to control the length of each trial and to practise the movements that are
harder, for example. A simple strategy to implement an unsupervised method would be
to ﬁrst classify the sample and then update the covariance matrix of the corresponding
trial, although studies have shown that such a method results in lower overall performance
[85]. Furthermore, this method requires that the initial accuracies are good enough, which
might not be the case with short training sessions. Nevertheless, this matter should be
investigated if the method is to be applied in real life.
A step necessary towards an automatic system is ﬁnding a strategy to determine the number
of samples needed for the convergence of the coeﬃcient estimates of the spatial ﬁlter. This
is important because: (a) it is not feasible to look at the convergence plots each time on
a practical application, and (b) the current method is an empirical procedure and depends
on who is handling the data. A possible procedure would be to set a threshold for the
oscillations of the eigenvalue estimates, but further analysis would be necessary on this topic
to determine if this is an appropriate procedure and how to set the threshold. Additionally,
the relatively large number of trials that the ﬁlter needs to converge is a disadvantage of
the method.
It is also desirable to compare the performance of the sample-wise adaptive CSP developed
in this project with the other existing adaptive methods, presented in section 4.3, and
investigate whether the results are comparable or if the apparent convergence issues of our
approach lead to worse performances. Furthermore, even smaller training sizes should be
considered in future experiments, given the results obtained by varying the training size in
the BCI competition data.
Finally, it would be desirable to achieve better overall performances. To do so, we propose
diﬀerent strategies to be investigated:
1. Diﬀerent electrode placement.
In our experiment, it was decided to simply place the EEG electrodes over the sensori-
motor cortex. Given that the motor tasks were all executed with one limb, placing the
electrodes only on the contralateral hemisphere, as in [59] could increase the spatial
resolution and, therefore, the classiﬁcation performances. Another possibility would
be to place electrodes over other areas of the brain, such as the frontal lobe (respon-
sible for decision making upon receiving the stimulus), the temporal and occipital
lobes (responsible for processing auditory and visual stimulus) and the parietal lobe
(as the somato-sensory cortex is also involded in motor tasks). Satisfactory results
have been obtained in the studies by [58, 60] through such electrode placements.
76
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 8. DISCUSSION
2. Combining the adaptation with a better performing version of CSP.
In this project we have proven that the adaptive CSP could increase the classiﬁcation
performances by up to 4 times the ones obtained by its non-adaptive counterpart. It
would be relevant to investigate the eﬀect of creating a similar adaptive version of a
more complex, better performing algorithm, such as the CSP.
3. Combining ACSP with an adaptive classiﬁer.
As stated previously, an adaptive classiﬁer was not implemented in this project for
lack of time and because the focus was on the adaptive spatial ﬁlter, but it is likely
that it would increase the overall classiﬁcation performances.
77
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
The aim of this project was to investigate the feasibility of a 3-class MI-based BCI system
which could be employed as an enhancement to current stroke rehabilitation therapies. Fur-
thermore, a strategy to overcome one of the disadvantages of current BCI paradigms, which
is the need of long training sessions to calibrate the system’s parameters, was investigated.
For this purpose, a sample-wise adaptive version of the spatial ﬁlter CSP based on RLS
updates of the ﬁlter coeﬃcients was implemented and tested.
In the process of developing the ﬁnal system, the algorithm was tested on existing data
belonging to the BCI competition IV: a 4-class problem comprising of imaginary movements
from diﬀerent body parts. The classiﬁcation performance of the adaptive algorithm was
obtained using a subject-dependent strategy and compared to that of its ﬁxed counterpart
and to the competition winner. Overall, the adaptive ﬁlter performed better than the ﬁxed
one, but not signiﬁcantly, and both performed worse than the winning algorithm. These
results indicate that the adaptation mechanism is not of the highest importance when there
is enough training data to compute robust CSP weights.
To investigate the eﬀect that smaller training sizes have on the algorithms’ performances,
simulated online sessions with single-trial classiﬁcation were performed on the BCI competi-
tion data with varying training lengths. The results indicated that the adaptive algorithm
trained on little data gives comparable performances to the ﬁxed CSP trained on large
amounts of data, which has steered the design of our own experiment.
Finally, EEG data was acquired from 14 healthy subjects in a 3-class problem where three
diﬀerent motor tasks were given for the right upper limb: palmar grasp, pinch and elbow
ﬂexion. Both movement imagery (MI) and execution (ME) were recorded. Relatively small
training sessions were carried out, in an attempt to test the feasibility of personalized
rehabilitation programs using a BCI system. For this problem, a regularized version of
the CSP algorithm using diagonal loading to shrink the covariance matrices towards the
identity matrix was used, in an attempt to robustify the computation of the CSP ﬁlters,
which is a process highly sensitive to outliers.
The results indicated that such a system is indeed feasible, with an average maximum
78
==PAGE:93==
CHAPTER 9. CONCLUSION
kappa value of 0.472 obtained for the MI task and 0.420 for the movement execution task.
The adaptive ﬁlter performed almost always better than its ﬁxed counterpart, leading to
a 4-fold and 1.5-fold increase on the average kappa value, respectively. As expected, the
classiﬁcation performances are lower in the second dataset, conﬁrming that it is a harder
task to distinguish between motor tasks performed by the same limb, particularly due to the
low spatial resolution of EEG. Surprisingly, lower accuracies were seen for the movement
execution tasks, which is contrary to the trend seen in the literature, where ME tends to
perform similarly or slightly better than MI. Overall, the results indicated a certain bias
of the classiﬁer towards elbow ﬂexion, which suggests that a fourth class corresponding to
the idling state should be included in such paradigms.
Finally, the advantages of using the regularized version of the CSP algorithm when there
is little training data were conﬁrmed in the MI task, but surprisingly not in the ME task,
where it performed worse, although not signiﬁcantly so. Overall, the results from the ME
tasks were lower than expected, indicating perhaps some higher level of noise in the data.
Overall, the feasibility of our paradigm was conﬁrmed, and the results suggest that the
implemented adaptive spatial ﬁlter can be of use towards individualized BCI systems for
stroke rehabilitation. It proved particularly useful when the ﬁlter coeﬃcients were obtained
on little training data, and not in the case when it is trained on large amounts of data. Even
though the results were positive, there is still much room for improvement towards practical
applicability, namely concerning channel reduction and development of an unsupervised
version of the algorithm.

B.1.2
K-fold cross-validation
In this section, we present the code (Matlab scripts) used to train the data for both datasets.
The loading of the data is the only diﬀerent part since the datasets have diﬀerent formats.
The toolbox Biosig [116] is used for classiﬁcation and the regularized CSPs come from the
RCSP toolbox for Matlab [87].

function

B.4

oped in this project.
